بيان النسب الأربعة ونقيضاهما بالعكس، أيضاً متساويان أي كل ما صدق عليه أحد النقيضين صدق عليه نقيض الآخر؛ إذ لو صدق أحدهما بدون الآخر لصدق مع عين الآخر؛ ضرورة استحالة ارتفاع النقيضين فيصدق عين الآخر بدون عين الأول؛ لامتناع اجتماع النقيضين وهذا يرفع التساوي بين العينين، مثلا: لو صدق اللا إنسان على شيء و لم يصدق عليه اللاناطق لصدق عليه الناطق فيصدق الناطق ههنا بدون الإنسان هذا خلف قوله ونقيضاهما بالعكس أي نقيض الأعم والأخص مطلقا أعم وأخص مطلقا لكن بعكس العينين فنقيض الأعم أخص، ونقيض الأخص أعم، يعني كل ما صدق عليه نقيض الأعم صدق عليه نقيض الأخص، وليس كل ما صدق عليه نقيض الأخص صدق عليه نقيض الأعم. الصدق ارتفاع النقيضين يعني أو لم يصدق مع عين الآخر أيضاً كما أنه لا يصدق مع نقيضه لزم ارتفاع النقيضين. فإن قلت إن ارتفاع النقيضين نقيض للنقيضين مع أنهما مرتفعان من كل شيء؛ إذ استحالتهما يمنع من على شيء في نفس الأمر، فارتفاع النقيضين ثابت في نفس الأمر وليس بمحال قلت مراد كم من النقيضين ما ذا أما النقيضان على سبيل الاجتماع فحينئذٍ لا نسلم أن ارتفاع النقيضين بهذا المعنى محال، ولا ندعي بطلانه، إنما المحال هو ارتفاع النقيضين بمعنى ارتفاع كل واحد وأما النقيضان على الترديد فنختار أن ارتفاع النقيضين بهذا المعنى مما ندعي استحالته لكن لا نسلم أن النقيضين بهذا المعنى باطل؛ فإن الباطل هو اجتماع النقيضين. فافهم (إسماعيل لامتناع أي لو صدق عين الآخر مع عين الأول لزم اجتماع النقيضين، وهو ممتنع. صدق اللاإنسان ليس بلا ناطق يستلزم صدق اللا إنسان ناطق. فيه نظر؛ فإن القضية الأولى سالبة معدولة والثانية موجبة محصلة وقد تقرر في موضعه أن السالبة المعدولة أعم من الموجبة المحصلة منها فكيف تستلزمها؟ والجواب بأن الأولى وإن كانت أعم من الثانية إلا أنهما متساويان عند وجود الموضوع وههنا كذلك الموجود اللا إنسان كالفرس. أخص كالحيوان مثلا أخص ونقيض الأخص كاللا إنسان أعم، فكل ما صدق عليه اللاحيوان كالحجر صدق عليه اللاإنسان، وليس كل ما صدق عليه اللاإنسان كالفرس صدق عليه اللاحيوان. (برهان)
Explanation of the four relations: and their opposites are reversed, also they are equal, meaning everything that one of the two opposites applies to, the opposite of the other applies to; because if one of them applied without the other, then it would apply with the other itself; necessarily due to the impossibility of the elevation of the two opposites, so the other itself applies without the first; due to the impossibility of the conjunction of the two opposites, and this removes the equality between the two selves. For example: if 'not-human' applies to something and 'not-rational' does not apply to it, then 'rational' applies to it, so 'rational' here applies without 'human' – this is a contrary. His statement: 'and their opposites are reversed' means the opposite of the absolutely general and the absolutely particular are absolutely general and absolutely particular but reversed with respect to the two selves. So the opposite of the general is particular, and the opposite of the particular is general, meaning everything that the opposite of the general applies to, the opposite of the particular applies to, but not everything that the opposite of the particular applies to does the opposite of the general apply to. Truth: the elevation of the two opposites means either it does not apply with the other itself, just as it does not apply with its opposite, then the elevation of the two opposites is necessary. If you say: The elevation of the two opposites is the opposite of the two opposites while they are elevated from everything; because their impossibility prevents [anything] from applying to a thing in reality, so the elevation of the two opposites is established in reality and is not impossible. I say: What do you mean by 'the two opposites'? If you mean the two opposites collectively, then we do not agree that the elevation of the two opposites in this sense is impossible, nor do we claim its falsity; the impossible is the elevation of the two opposites in the sense of the elevation of each one. If you mean the two opposites disjunctively, then we choose that the elevation of the two opposites in this sense is something we claim is impossible, but we do not agree that the two opposites in this sense are false; for the false is the conjunction of the two opposites. So understand. (Isma'il) Regarding the impossibility: i.e., if the other itself applied with the first itself, the conjunction of the two opposites would follow, which is impossible. The truth of 'not-human' [being] not 'not-rational' entails the truth of 'not-human rational'. There is consideration: for the first proposition is a negative privative and the second is a positive unqualified, and it has been established in its place that the negative privative is more general than the positive unqualified, so how can it entail it? The answer is that the first, although it is more general than the second, yet they are equal when the subject exists, and here it is likewise; the existence of 'not-human' like the horse. A particular like animal, for example, is particular, and the opposite of the particular like 'not-human' is general, so everything that 'not-animal' applies to, like stone, 'not-human' applies to, but not everything that 'not-human' applies to, like horse, does 'not-animal' apply to. (Proof)
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the rule of opposites (نقيض) in the context of the four relations between universal terms. The author begins by stating the rule for equal terms (متساويان): if two terms are equal, their opposites are also equal. The proof is given: if one opposite applied without the other, then the other itself would apply without the first, contradicting the original equality. An example is given: if 'not-human' applies to something but 'not-rational' does not, then 'rational' applies, which means 'rational' applies without 'human', which is impossible if 'human' and 'rational' are equal. 

Next, the author turns to the relation of absolute generality/specificity (عموم وخصوص مطلق). The rule is that the opposite of the more general is more specific, and the opposite of the more specific is more general. This reverses the original relation. For instance, if 'animal' is more general than 'human', then 'not-animal' is more specific than 'not-human'. The proof is implied: everything that 'not-animal' applies to (e.g., stone) also 'not-human' applies to, but not vice versa (horse is not-human but is animal).

A subtle objection is raised regarding the concept of 'elevation of contradictories' (ارتفاع النقيضين). The objector claims that if both contradictories can be false (as in the case of 'not-human' and 'not-rational' both being true of a horse), then the elevation of contradictories is possible. The answer distinguishes two meanings of 'contradictories': if meant collectively (both false at once), that is impossible; if meant disjunctively (each individually false), that is possible, but that should not be called elevation of contradictories in the technical sense. The marginal note by Isma'il further discusses the logical equivalence of a negative privative and a positive unqualified under the condition of existing subject.

This lesson emphasizes that the behavior of opposites is not arbitrary but follows logical rules derived from the relations of the original terms.

Key Terms
النقيض
الأعم المطلق
الأخص المطلق
ارتفاع النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Equal terms have equal opposites, proven by the impossibility of contradiction.

Opposites of General and Specific Terms
Author explains the inversion rule for opposites of general and specific terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the relation between opposites of general and specific terms using the inversion rule: opposite of general is specific, opposite of specific is general.

Core Idea
The opposite of the general is specific, and vice versa — a reversal of order.

When one term is absolutely more general than another, their opposites do not preserve the same relation. Instead, the relation reverses. This lesson explains the inversion rule and its logical basis, using examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general and specific terms
Find a pair where one term is absolutely more general than the other, e.g., 'animal' (عام) and 'human' (خاص). The general applies to everything the specific applies to, but not vice versa.
2
State the inversion rule
The author says: 'the opposite of the absolutely general is absolutely particular, and the opposite of the absolutely particular is absolutely general, but reversed with respect to the two selves.' This means the directional relation flips.
3
Test with extensions
Check that every instance of 'not-animal' (e.g., stone) is also 'not-human', but not every instance of 'not-human' (e.g., horse) is 'not-animal'. This confirms the inversion.
4
Apply to a new pair
Consider 'body' (جسم) and 'animal' (حيوان) in Islamic philosophy. Determine the relation between 'not-body' and 'not-animal' using the inversion rule.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the rule of opposites (نقيض) in the context of the four relations between universal terms. The author begins by stating the rule for equal terms (متساويان): if two terms are equal, their opposites are also equal. The proof is given: if one opposite applied without the other, then the other itself would apply without the first, contradicting the original equality. An example is given: if 'not-human' applies to something but 'not-rational' does not, then 'rational' applies, which means 'rational' applies without 'human', which is impossible if 'human' and 'rational' are equal. 

Next, the author turns to the relation of absolute generality/specificity (عموم وخصوص مطلق). The rule is that the opposite of the more general is more specific, and the opposite of the more specific is more general. This reverses the original relation. For instance, if 'animal' is more general than 'human', then 'not-animal' is more specific than 'not-human'. The proof is implied: everything that 'not-animal' applies to (e.g., stone) also 'not-human' applies to, but not vice versa (horse is not-human but is animal).

A subtle objection is raised regarding the concept of 'elevation of contradictories' (ارتفاع النقيضين). The objector claims that if both contradictories can be false (as in the case of 'not-human' and 'not-rational' both being true of a horse), then the elevation of contradictories is possible. The answer distinguishes two meanings of 'contradictories': if meant collectively (both false at once), that is impossible; if meant disjunctively (each individually false), that is possible, but that should not be called elevation of contradictories in the technical sense. The marginal note by Isma'il further discusses the logical equivalence of a negative privative and a positive unqualified under the condition of existing subject.

This lesson emphasizes that the behavior of opposites is not arbitrary but follows logical rules derived from the relations of the original terms.

Opposites of Partially Overlapping Universals: Partial Difference
Explains the opposite rule for partial overlap and absolute difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the relation between the opposites of two universals given their original relation as partial overlap or absolute difference, and construct a proof for partial difference.

Core Idea
When two terms partially overlap, their opposites are not equal but have a partial difference.

In the previous lesson we learned that for terms with absolute generality/specificity, their opposites have absolute generality/specificity reversed. Now we turn to the remaining two relations: partial overlap (من وجه) and absolute difference (تباين). Surprisingly, both yield the same relation between opposites: partial difference (تباين جزئي). This lesson will walk through the proof and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Recall the four relations: equality, absolute generality/specificity, partial overlap, and absolute difference. Here we focus on the last two: when terms coincide only partially (من وجه) or not at all (تباين كلّي). The passage states that in both cases, the relation between their opposites is partial difference (تباين جزئي).
Partial difference means the opposites are not equal, nor does one subsume the other entirely.
2
Understand the main claim
The author asserts: 'وإلا فمن وجه وبين نقيضيهما تباين جزئي' – otherwise (if not absolute generality/specificity), then it is partial overlap, and between their opposites is partial difference. This is the rule to be proven.
The phrase 'وإلا' refers back to the previous case (absolute generality/specificity).
3
Follow the reductio proof
Consider the example: 'لا حيوان' (not-animal) and 'لا إنسان' (not-human). Assume the opposite of the general (not-animal) applies to something but the opposite of the particular (not-human) does not. That something would then be human (since not not-human = human). But then human applies without animal, contradicting the original relation that animal is more general (every human is an animal). Thus the initial assumption is false.
This proof shows that the opposite of the general must be coextensive with the opposite of the particular under the condition of absolute generality/specificity; here it is used to contrast with partial overlap.
4
Apply the rule to partial overlap
When the original terms have partial overlap (e.g., 'أبيض' (white) and 'إنسان' (human)), their opposites cannot have absolute generality/specificity or equality. The passage demonstrates that they must have partial difference. Walk through the logic: if the opposites were equal, then the original terms would be equal (by the rule that opposites of equals are equal), which is false.
Use the example given: 'لا أبيض' and 'لا إنسان' have partial difference.
5
Verify with a concrete instance
Take a specific object, e.g., a white horse. It is not-human (if we consider human as species), but it is not-white? Actually a white horse is white, so it does not fall under 'not-white'. Pick an object that is not-white and not-human, like a black stone. It is not-human and not-white. Both opposites apply. But consider a white human: it is human, so not-human does not apply, but not-white? It is white, so not-white does not apply. So the opposites do not completely coincide; some objects are one without the other, hence partial difference.
This confirms the relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التصورات بيان النسب الأربعة وإلا فمن وجه وبين نقيضيهما تباين جزئي أما الأول؛ فلأنه لو صدق نقيض الأعم على شيء بدون نقيض الأخص لصدق عين الأخص، فيصدق عين الأخص بدون عين الأعم، هذا خلف مثلا لو صدق اللاحيوان على شيء بدون اللاإنسان لصدق عليه الإنسان عينه، ويمتنع هناك صدق الحيوان؛ لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين، فيصدق الإنسان بدون الحيوان. وأما الثاني؛ فلأنه بعد ما ثبت أن كل نقيض الأعم نقيض الأخص لو كان كل نقيض الأخص نقيض الأعم لكان النقيضان متساويين، فيكون نقيضاهما وهما العينان متساويين؛ لما مر وقد كان العينان أعم وأخص مطلقا، هذا خلف. قوله: وإلا فمن وجه أي وإن لم يتصادقا كليا من جانبين أو من جانب واحد قوله تباين جزئي التباين الجزئي: الأول: توضيحه أنه كل ما صدق عليه نقيض الأخص الأعم، فلابد أن يصدق مع عين الأخص، فيصدق حينئذ عين الأخص بدون عين الأعم؛ لاستحالة اجتماع النقيضين، فنقول : كل ما صدق اللاحيوان عليه ولا يصدق عليه اللا إنسان فيصدق عليه الإنسان للزوم ارتفاع النقيضين فيصدق الإنسان عليه بدون الحيوان؛ لفرض صدق اللاحيوان واستحالة اجتماع النقيضين وهذا خلاف المفروض؛ فإنا قد فرضنا بينهما عموما وخصوصا مطلقا، فيصدق الحيوان على كل ما يصدق عليه الإنسان دون العكس فافهم (إسماعيل ولأنه لو لم يصدق كل ما صدق عليه نقيض الأعم صدق عليه نقيض الأخص لصدق نقيضه، وهو ليس كل ما صدق عليه نقيض الأعم صدق عليه نقيض الأخص، وهو يستلزم بعض ما صدق عليه نقيض الأعم صدق عليه نقيض الأخص، وينعكس إلى بعض ما صدق عليه نقيض الأخص صدق عليه نقيض الأعم، فيلزم صدق الأخص بدون الأعم، وهذا محال (عبد الثاني وهو ليس كل ما يصدق نقيض الأخص صدق عليه نقيض الأعم. ما ثبت توضيحه أن نقيض الأخص أعم من نقيض الأعم بمعنى أنه ليس كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأخص : يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم؛ فإنه قد ثبت أنه يصدق نقيض الأخص على كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم، فلو كان نقيض الأعم أيضا يصدق على كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأخص لكان بين النقيضين تساو؛ لتصادقهما كليا على هذا التقدير وقد مر أيضًا أن نقيضي المتساويين يكونان متساويين، فيكون نقيضا هذين النقيضين أي العينان متساويين وقد فرض بينهما عموم وخصوص مطلقا . هذا خلف.
The concepts: Explanation of the four relations: otherwise, it is from one aspect (من وجه), and between their opposites is partial difference (تباين جزئي). As for the first: because if the opposite of the general applied to something without the opposite of the particular, the particular itself would apply, so the particular itself applies without the general; this is a contrary. For example, if 'not-animal' applied to something without 'not-human', then 'human' itself would apply to it, and the application of 'animal' there would be impossible due to the impossibility of the conjunction of the two opposites; thus 'human' applies without 'animal'. As for the second: because after it is established that every opposite of the general is an opposite of the particular, if every opposite of the particular were an opposite of the general, then the two opposites would be equal; thus their opposites, which are the two selves, would be equal; as has been mentioned, yet the two selves were absolutely general and particular; this is a contrary. His statement: 'otherwise, it is from one aspect' meaning if they do not completely coincide from two sides or from one side. His statement: 'partial difference': partial difference: the first: its clarification is that everything that the opposite of the particular applies to, it must apply with the particular itself, so then the particular itself applies without the general; due to the impossibility of the conjunction of the two opposites. So we say: everything that 'not-animal' applies to and 'not-human' does not apply to, then 'human' applies to it due to the necessity of the elevation of the two opposites; thus 'human' applies to it without 'animal'; due to the assumption of the truth of 'not-animal' and the impossibility of the conjunction of the two opposites. And this is contrary to what was assumed; for we assumed between them absolute generality and particularity, so that 'animal' applies to everything that 'human' applies to, but not vice versa. So understand. (Isma'il) And because if it were not the case that everything that the opposite of the general applies to also has the opposite of the particular apply, then its opposite would apply, which is 'not everything that the opposite of the general applies to has the opposite of the particular apply to it', and this entails that some things that the opposite of the general applies to have the opposite of the particular apply, and it reflects to some things that the opposite of the particular applies to having the opposite of the general apply; so the particular applies without the general follows, and this is impossible. (Abd al-Thani) And it is not that everything that the opposite of the particular applies to has the opposite of the general apply. What has been established is its clarification that the opposite of the particular is more general than the opposite of the general, in the sense that not everything that the opposite of the particular applies to has the opposite of the general apply; for it has been established that the opposite of the particular applies to everything that the opposite of the general applies to. If the opposite of the general also applied to everything that the opposite of the particular applies to, then there would be equality between the two opposites; due to their complete coincidence on this assumption. And it has also been mentioned that the opposites of equals are equal, so the opposites of these two opposites, i.e., the two selves, would be equal, yet absolute generality and particularity was assumed between them. This is a contrary.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage continues the explanation of the four relations, focusing on the cases of partial overlap (من وجه) and absolute difference (تباين). The author first addresses the relation of absolute generality/specificity (which was the main topic earlier) and provides two proofs that the opposites are not equal. The first proof shows that if the opposite of the general applied without the opposite of the particular, then the particular would apply without the general, which contradicts the original relation. The example uses 'not-animal' and 'not-human': if something is not-animal but not not-human (i.e., it is human), then human applies without animal, which is impossible if animal is more general. The second proof shows that if every opposite of the particular were also an opposite of the general, then the two opposites would be equal, leading to the original terms being equal, contrary to the assumed generality/specificity.

The author then clarifies the phrase 'وإلا فمن وجه' (otherwise, from one aspect), meaning that if the terms only partially coincide (some overlap, but not complete from both sides), then the relation between their opposites is partial difference (تباين جزئي). This is further explained with a detailed proof: if something is not-animal but not not-human (i.e., it is human), then human applies without animal, violating absolute generality/specificity. The marginal notes by Isma'il and Abd al-Thani provide additional logical justifications, showing that if the opposites were equal, the original terms would be equal, which is false.

Thus, the lesson concludes that for terms that are partially overlapping or absolutely different, their opposites also have a specific relation: partial difference, meaning they are not interchangeable but have some exclusion.

Key Takeaway
For terms with partial overlap or absolute difference, their opposites are in a relation of partial difference: each opposite excludes some instances of the other, but not all.

Partial Contrast and Its Negations
Author defines partial contrast, explains its occurrence within other relations, and discusses the negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish partial contrast from other universal relations and determine the relation between the negations of two universals given their original relation.

Core Idea
Partial contrast is not a fifth relation but a subtype of general-particular or total contrast.

In logic, we classify relations between universals. One of these is 'partial contrast', which occurs when each universal applies without the other in some cases. This passage explains that partial contrast is not a separate relation but is realized within general-particular (عموم من وجه) or total contrast (تباين كلي). It also explores what happens when we negate such universals. Understanding this is crucial for precise reasoning in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four universal relations
From the passage, list the four relations: equality, general-particular, partial contrast, total contrast. Note how partial contrast is defined as each applying without the other.
The passage says partial contrast occurs when each applies without the other in general, but may apply together or never.
2
See how partial contrast fits within other relations
The author states that partial contrast is realized within general-particular (if they sometimes apply together) or within total contrast (if they never apply together). So it is not a fifth independent relation.
For example, 'salat' and 'sawm' have a general-particular relation, thus also partial contrast. 'wujud' and 'adam' have total contrast, thus also partial contrast.
3
Analyze the negations of general-particular terms
Take two universals with general-particular relation, e.g., 'alim' and 'qadir' (knowing and powerful). Their negations ('non-knowing' and 'non-powerful') may also have general-particular or total contrast. The passage uses 'animal' and 'white' as example.
The negations of general-particular terms can themselves be in general-particular (like non-animal and non-white) or in total contrast (like non-animal and human).
4
Analyze the negations of total contrast terms
Take two universals in total contrast, e.g., 'wujud' and 'adam' (existence and non-existence). Their negations ('non-existence' and 'non-non-existence') also have a partial contrast, which may be total contrast or general-particular.
The passage explains that the negations of total contrast terms always have partial contrast, encompassing both possibilities.
5
Synthesize the rule for negations
Combine the observations: For any two universals in general-particular or total contrast, their negations always have a partial contrast. This is why the term 'partial contrast' was chosen for the negations.
Thus, whether original universals are in general-particular or total contrast, the negations are always in partial contrast.

التصورات بيان النسب الأربعة هو صدق كل من الكليين بدون الآخر في الجملة، فإن صدقا أيضًا معا كان بينهما عموم من وجه، وإن لم يتصادقا معا أصلا كان بينهما تباين كلي. فالتباين الجزئي يتحقق في ضمن العموم من وجه وفي ضمن التباين الكلي أيضًا، ثم إنّ الأمرين اللذين بينهما عموم من وجه قد يكون بين نقيضيهما العموم من وجه أيضًا، كالحيوان والأبيض، فإن بين نقيضيهما وهما اللاحيوان واللا أبيض أيضًا عموما من وجه، وقد يكون بين نقيضيهما تباين كلي كالحيوان واللاإنسان؛ فإن بينهما عموما من وجه وبين نقيضيهما وهما اللاحيوان والإنسان مباينة كلية فلهذا قالوا: إن بين نقيضي الأعم والأخص من وجه تباينا جزئيا لا العموم من وجه فقط ولا التباين الكلي فقط. قوله: كالمتباينين: فلا يرد في الجملة أي سواء لم يتصادقا أصلا أو لم يتصادقا في بعض وتصادقا في بعض آخر . (تحفه هذا: مفعول لفعل محذوف فاعله أي "خذ هذا". هذا هو المشهور، وقد قيل: إن "ها" اسم فعل بمعنى "خذ" و "ذا" اسمه المنصوب محلا، فهذا وإن كان مما يأباه رسم الخط إلا أن فيه سلامة عن الحذف. إسماعيل) واعلم جواب عما يقال : إنه لم لم يذكر النسبة بين نقيضي المتباينين عقيبهما؟
The concepts: Explanation of the four relations. It is that each of the two universals applies without the other in general, whether they also apply together—then there is a general-particular relation—or they never apply together at all—then there is total contrast. Partial contrast is realized within general-particular and also within total contrast. Then, for two things that have a general-particular relation, their negations may also have a general-particular relation, like animal and white; for their negations, namely non-animal and non-white, also have a general-particular relation. Or their negations may have a total contrast, like animal and non-human; for they have a general-particular relation, while their negations, non-animal and human, have a total contrast. Hence they said: Between the negations of the more general and more particular with respect to something there is a partial contrast, not only general-particular nor only total contrast. His statement: 'like the two contrasting ones': so it is not objected. 'In general' i.e., whether they never apply together or they do not apply in some cases and apply in others. (Abd) And whether they both apply together as well, as each applies without the other, or they never apply together at all. In the first case the relation between them is general-particular; in the second, total contrast. So partial contrast is general-particular or total contrast, [meaning] that partial contrast is another relation aside from the mentioned relations, thus invalidating the restriction to four. (Ismail) 'They apply' i.e., while each applies without the other. (Abd) Like animal and white: the relation between them is general-particular, and between their negations, i.e., non-animal and non-white, is also general-particular; for they both apply together in a matter like black stone, and non-animal applies without non-white in white stone, and non-white applies without non-animal in black animal, like the crow. (Ismail) 'general-particular' because they both apply in black stone and separate in paper and crow. (Abd) Like animal and non-human: there is a general-particular relation between them, since each applies in the horse, and animal applies without non-human in Zayd, and non-human applies without animal in the stone. (Ismail) 'Total contrast' necessarily because it is impossible for the specific to apply without the general. (Abd) 'Hence': i.e., because between the negations of the two things that have a general-particular relation there may be general-particular or total contrast, they chose the term 'partial contrast' encompassing both; it violates the rule in some matters by mentioning one without the other. (Ismail) 'Lest it be objected': 'like the two contrasting ones': the intent is to liken the negations of the more general and more particular with respect to something to the negations of the two contrasting ones, as the context requires. That is, just as between the negations of the more general and more particular with respect to something there is a partial contrast, so too between the negations of the two contrasting ones there is a partial contrast. For when each of the two eyes applies with the negation of the other, each of the two negations applies with the other eye; thus each of the two negations applies without the other in general, which is partial contrast. Then, it may be realized within total contrast, like existence and non-existence; for between their negations, namely non-existence and non-non-existence, there is a total contrast. And it may be realized within general-particular, like man and stone; for between their negations—non-man and non-stone—there is a general-particular relation. Hence they said that between their negations there is a partial contrast, so that it holds in all cases. Know also that the author delayed mentioning the negations of the two contrasting ones for two reasons: first, for brevity. i.e., Know that the author's expression requires that the negations of the two contrasting ones be the similitude, and the negations of the more general and more particular with respect to something be the similized; while the commentator's expression requires the opposite: the negations of the more general and more particular are the similized, and the negations of the two contrasting ones are the similitude. Perhaps the reason is that if the negations of the two contrasting ones were the similitude, as understood from the apparent meaning of his expression, then it would require that it be mentioned before the similitude, but it is not so; therefore the commentator reversed it. Partial contrast: And the secret is that the two eyes do not apply together—e.g., man does not apply with stone, nor stone with man. So when each applies with the negation of the other: for example, if man does not apply with stone, it must apply with the negation of stone, i.e., non-stone, otherwise it would necessitate the two negations both false. Similarly, stone, if it does not apply with man, inevitably applies with non-man, because it is impossible for both to be false. And if each of the two contrasting ones applies with the negation of the other, then each of the two negations does not apply with the other eye. But if each of the two negations applies with the other eye, then each applies without the other, and that state is partial contrast. Total contrast: i.e., the separation between the two universals in all matters, and within it is realized separation in some matters as well, which is partial contrast. 'A total contrast': for non-existence is effectively non-existent and non-non-existence is effectively existence, so it is impossible for each of them to apply to the other, otherwise it would necessitate that one thing be both existent and non-existent together, which is impossible. (Ismail) 'A general-particular relation' because they both apply in tree and separate in this stone and Zayd. (Tuhfa: This is an object for an omitted verb whose subject is 'take this'. This is the well-known view. It was also said that 'ha' is a verb meaning 'take' and 'dha' is its object in the accusative. This, although it violates orthography, is free from ellipsis. (Ismail) 'And know' is a response to the question: why did he not mention the relation between the negations of the two contrasting ones immediately after them?
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the four relations between universals, focusing on partial contrast. It begins by defining the four relations: equality, general-particular, partial contrast, and total contrast. The definition given is that partial contrast occurs when each universal applies without the other in some cases, but they may also apply together or never. The author clarifies that partial contrast is not a fifth relation but is realized within general-particular (عموم من وجه) or total contrast (تباين كلي). The commentator then discusses the negations of terms that have a general-particular relation, using examples like animal and white. Their negations (non-animal and non-white) also have a general-particular relation. In contrast, animal and non-human have a general-particular relation, but their negations (non-animal and human) have a total contrast. The commentator explains that the term 'partial contrast' was chosen to encompass both possibilities: when the negations have general-particular or total contrast. The passage then turns to the negations of two totally contrasting terms, like human and stone. The commentator shows that the negations of such terms also have a partial contrast, and this can occur within total contrast (e.g., existence and non-existence) or within general-particular (e.g., human and stone). Finally, there is a note on the author's ordering: why the author delayed mentioning the negations of the two contrasting terms. The commentator suggests it was for brevity and discusses the symmetry between the two cases.

Defining the Relative Particular and Expanding 'the More Specific'
Author defines relative particular and defends against an objection about its definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relative particular (al-juz'i al-idafi) and explain why the term 'the more specific' (al-akhas) is used in a broader sense to include both universals and real particulars.

Core Idea
The relative particular is 'the more specific' but in a broader sense than before.

In this passage, the author introduces the second application of 'particular', known as the relative particular. He defines it as 'the more specific' (al-akhas), but this seems to conflict with an earlier definition that restricted 'the more specific' to universals. The author resolves this by clarifying that here 'the more specific' is used in a more general sense, encompassing both real particulars and relative particulars. This subtle expansion is crucial for correctly applying the five universals in definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'relative particular' (al-juz'i al-idafi) in the Arabic. The author defines it as 'the more specific' (al-akhas).
The definition is straightforward: relative particular = the more specific.
2
Recognize the implied objection
The author anticipates an objection: previously, 'the more specific' was defined as a universal that is predicated of another universal (and not vice versa). But the relative particular can be a real particular, which is not a universal. So using the old definition would not cover real particulars.
The objection is implicit in the author's response: 'and it is more general'.
3
Extract the author's expansion
The author responds by saying 'and it is more general' (wa huwa a'am), meaning that the 'more specific' in this context is broader than the earlier definition. It now includes both universals and real particulars.
This expansion allows the definition to be comprehensive.
4
Understand the consequence
Because the term is broader, the relative particular is more general than the real particular. The relation between them is understood implicitly from the definition.
This shows the hierarchy: relative particular > real particular in generality.

الإطلاق الثاني للجزئي ولك أن تحمل قوله : وهو أعم على جواب سؤال مقدر كأن قائلا يقول: الأخص على ما عُلم سابقاً هو الكلي الذي يصدق عليه كلي آخر صدقا كليا ولا يصدق هو على ذلك الآخر كذلك، والجزئي الإضافي لا يلزم أن يكون كليا، بل قد يكون جزئيا حقيقيا، فتفسير الجزئي الإضافي بالأخص بهذا المعنى تفسير بالأخص فأجاب بقوله : وهو أعم أي الأخص المذكور ههنا أعم من المعلوم سابقا، ومنه يعلم أن الجزئي بهذا المعنى أعم من الجزئي الحقيقي، فيعلم بيان النسبة التزاما. وهذا من فوائد بعض مشايخنا طاب الله ثراه
The second application of the particular. You may carry his saying: 'and it is more general' as an answer to an implied question, as if someone says: 'The more specific, as previously known, is the universal which is predicated of another universal with a complete predication and that other is not predicated of it likewise, and the relative particular does not necessarily have to be a universal, but may be a real particular. So interpreting the relative particular by 'the more specific' in this sense is an interpretation by the more specific.' So he answered by his saying: 'and it is more general', meaning that the more specific mentioned here is more general than what was previously known, and from it it is known that the particular in this sense is more general than the real particular, so the relation is known implicitly. And this is among the benefits of some of our shaykhs, may God moisten his grave.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage defines the second application of the term 'particular' (al-juz'i), which is the relative particular (al-juz'i al-idafi). The author states that this relative particular is defined as 'the more specific' (al-akhas). However, a potential objection is raised: previously, 'the more specific' was defined as a universal that is predicated of another universal, but the relative particular can sometimes be a real particular (which is not a universal). So defining relative particular by 'the more specific' in the previous sense would be too narrow. The author responds by saying 'and it is more general' (wa huwa a'am), meaning that the term 'the more specific' here is used in a broader sense than before, encompassing both real particular and relative particular. This implies that the relative particular is more general than the real particular, and the relation between them is understood implicitly. The commentator notes that this is a benefit from some shaykhs. The explanation follows the order of the Arabic: first the definition, then the implied objection, then the answer, and finally the implied consequence.

The Five Universals: Genus, Species, and Hypothethicals
Author classifies the five universals, defines genus, and addresses a doubt about hypothetical universals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify genus and species within the five universals and explain why hypothetical universals are excluded from them.

Core Idea
The five universals only include those with actual individuals; hypothethicals are excluded.

In this passage, the author begins classifying the five universals, distinguishing between universals that have actual individuals (in the mind or externally) and those that do not. He then defines genus as a part of the essence of its individuals, contrasting it with species. A famous doubt arises: how can hypothethical universals, which are nothing, be considered universals at all? The author begins to answer by redefining 'thing' in the definition of conception. This lesson will guide you through the classification, definitions, and the initial resolution of the doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classification of universals
The passage states that universals (al-kulliyyat) are limited to five types, but only those with actual individuals (in mind or external world). Hypothetical universals with no instances are excluded from investigation.
2
Extract the definitions of genus and species
When a universal relates to its actual individuals, it is either the very essence of those individuals (species) or a part of that essence (genus). So genus is a partial essence, species is the complete essence.
These definitions are standard in the Islamic logical tradition.
3
Understand the famous doubt
The doubt questions whether hypothethical universals (like 'nothing') can be included under the universal. If they were universals, they would be 'things', but 'thing' applies only to existents, while hypothethicals are non-existent. This seems contradictory.
The doubt is a criticism of including hypothethicals in the five universals.
4
Analyze the beginning of the answer
The author reports a response: 'thing' in the definition of conception is taken to mean 'that which can be known and spoken about', which includes existents, non-existents, and even impossible entities. This allows hypothethicals to be considered 'things' in that sense.
The passage ends with 'so either there is a consideration in it', indicating further analysis.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكليات خمس: الأول: الجنس والكليات أي الكليات التي لها أفراد بحسب نفس الأمر في الذهن أو في الخارج منحصرة في خمسة أنواع. وأما الكليات الفرضية التي لا مصداق لها خارجا ولا ذهنا فلا يتعلق بالبحث عنها غرض يُعتد به. ثم الكلي إذا نسب إلى أفراده المحققة في نفس الأمر، فإما أن يكون عين حقيقة تلك الأفراد وهو النوع، أو جزء حقيقتها. وههنا شك مشهور وهو أن في اندراج الكليات الفرضية التي هي لا شيء تحت الكلي فسادا؛ إذ الكلي ما لا يمتنع تصوره عن الشركة والتصور هو حصول صورة الشيء في العقل، فلو كانت كليات لكانت أشياء، والشيء إنما يطلق على الموجود لا المعدوم، قيل: الشيء مأخوذ في تعريف التصور بمعنى ما يمكن أن يعلم ويخبر عنه وهو شامل للموجود والمعدوم واللاشيء واللا ممكن. فإما أن فيه نظر
And the universals are five: the first: genus. And the universals — i.e., the universals that have individuals in reality, in the mind or in the external world — are limited to five types. As for the hypothetical universals that have no instantiation externally or mentally, no worthwhile purpose is attached to researching them. Then the universal, when related to its individuals that are actualized in reality, is either the very essence of those individuals — and that is the species — or a part of their essence. And here there is a famous doubt, which is that there is corruption in including hypothetical universals that are nothing under the universal; because the universal is that whose conception does not prevent sharing, and conception is the occurrence of the image of a thing in the mind. So if they were universals, they would be things, and 'thing' is only applied to the existent, not the non-existent. It was said: 'thing' in the definition of conception is taken to mean that which can be known and spoken about, and it includes the existent, the non-existent, the nothing, and the impossible. So either there is a consideration in it.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage begins the classification of the five universals (al-kulliyyat al-khams). It defines that these five universals are those that have actual individuals either in the mind or in the external world. Hypothetical universals that have no instances are excluded from scientific investigation. Then it defines genus (al-jins) implicitly through the classification: when a universal is related to its actual individuals, it is either the very essence of those individuals (species) or a part of their essence (genus). The passage then raises a famous doubt (shakk mashhur) about including hypothetical universals. The doubt argues that if hypothetical universals are considered universals, they would be 'things', but 'thing' applies only to existents, while hypotheticals are non-existent. The response begins by redefining 'thing' in the definition of conception as 'that which can be known and spoken about', which includes non-existents. The passage ends with the beginning of a further analysis. The commentary follows the order: first the classification, then the exclusion of hypotheticals, then the definition of genus/species, then the doubt and initial answer.

The Five Universals and Definition of Genus
Defining the five universals and the genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the five universals and identify the genus in any given definition.

Core Idea
The five universals are the building blocks of all definitions in Islamic logic.

In this lesson, we delve into the foundational classification of universals in Islamic logic: the five universals. Understanding these categories is essential for constructing and analyzing definitions in any Islamic science. The passage defines each universal and provides a detailed definition of the genus, including its close and remote forms, which will be crucial for your later studies in definition theory and argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five universals
From the passage, list the five types of universals that have actual individuals: species, genus, differentia, property, and general accident. Note that hypothetical universals are excluded.
2
Distinguish essential vs. accidental
Notice that the first three (species, genus, differentia) are called 'essentials' (dhātiyyāt) because they are part of the essence, while the last two (property, general accident) are 'accidents' (ʿaraḍiyyāt).
3
Understand the definition of genus
The passage defines genus as 'that which is predicated of many differing in realities in answer to "What is it?"' This means the genus captures the shared essence. Study the examples of close genus (like 'animal' for humans) and remote genus (like 'growing body').
4
Apply the definition to new cases
Practice by determining the genus for other species, e.g., for horses, 'animal' is a close genus, 'living body' is remote. Check if the answer to 'What is it?' applies uniformly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكليات أي الكليات التي لها أفراد بحسب نفس الأمر في الذهن أو في الخارج منحصرة في خمسة أنواع. وأما الكليات الفرضية التي لا مصداق لها خارجا ولا ذهنا فلا يتعلق بالبحث عنها غرض يُعتد به. ثم الكلي إذا نسب إلى أفراده المحققة في نفس الأمر، فإما أن يكون عين حقيقة تلك الأفراد وهو النوع، أو جزء حقيقتها، وهو الجنس أو الفصل، فإن كان تمام المشترك بين شيء منها وبين بعض آخر فهو الجنس، وإلا فهو الفصل. ويقال لهذه الثلاثة ذاتيات، أو خارجا عنها ويقال له: العرضي، فإما أن يختص بأفراد حقيقةٍ واحدةٍ أو لا يختص، فالأول هو الخاصة والثاني هو العرض العام. فهذا دليل انحصار الكليات في الخمسة. تعريف الجنس: وهو المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالحقائق في جواب ما هو؟، فإن كان الجواب عن الماهية وعن بعض المشاركات هو الجواب عنها وعن الكل، فقريب كالحيوان، وإلا فبعيد، كالجسم النامي.
The universals—i.e., those universals that have individuals according to actual existence in the mind or in the external world—are confined to five types. As for the hypothetical universals that have no instantiation either externally or mentally, no worthwhile purpose is attached to investigating them. Then, when a universal is related to its actually existing individuals, it is either the very essence of those individuals—which is the species—or a part of their essence, which is the genus or the differentia. If it is the shared part between something and another, it is the genus; otherwise it is the differentia. These three are called essentials. Or it is external to them and is called the accident: it either is specific to individuals of a single essence—which is the property—or not specific, which is the general accident. This is the proof of the confinement of the universals to five. Definition of genus: It is that which is predicated of many differing in realities in answer to 'What is it?'. If the answer about the essence and about some of the participants is the same as the answer about it and about the whole, it is close like 'animal'; otherwise it is remote like 'growing body'.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage defines the five universals (al-kulliyāt al-khams) and provides the specific definition of genus (al-jins). The author begins by specifying that the universals under discussion are those that have actual individuals, whether in the mind or in the external world; hypothetical universals without instantiations are not considered. The classification proceeds: when a universal is related to its individuals, it is either the entire essence (species) or a part of the essence. If that part is completely shared by the individuals, it is genus; if it distinguishes them, it is differentia. These three (species, genus, differentia) are called essentials (dhātiyyāt). Anything else is accidental, which is either property (khāṣṣa) if specific to one essence, or general accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm) if not. This yields the enumeration of five universals. Then the definition of genus is given: it is predicated of many differing in realities in answer to 'What is it?'. The author clarifies that if the answer applies both to the species and to the whole, it is a close genus (like 'animal' for humans and horses); if not, it is a remote genus (like 'growing body' for plants and animals). The explanation helps distinguish genus from differentia and ensures proper classification.

Defining Genus and Its Types
Defines genus and divides it into close and distant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish close genus from distant genus and identify the complete common part.

Core Idea
Genus is not just any commonality; it is the complete common part in answer to 'what is it?'.

The concept of genus is foundational to the five universals. This passage gives it a precise definition and then divides it into close and distant. By understanding this division, you will be able to evaluate how well a universal term captures the essence of things. We will walk through the definition step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The passage begins by naming what it defines: 'al-jins' (الجنس). This is the term whose definition we are analyzing.
The definiendum is the term being defined.
2
Parse the definition
The definition states: 'al-maqul 'ala kathirin mukhtalifin bi al-haqa'iq fi jawab ma huwa' — It is a universal predicated of many different essences in answer to 'what is it?' This establishes that genus tells us what something is.
3
Distinguish close vs. distant
The passage then gives a condition: if the answer about the quiddity (essence) and about some participants is the same as the answer about the quiddity and about all participants, then it is close. Otherwise, it is distant. 'Close' here means the genus gives the same answer for both the species and the broader category.
For example, 'animal' answers what a human is and what a horse is identically, so it is close. 'Growing body' does not give the same answer for all participants — a plant is a growing body but not an animal — so it is distant.
4
Understand 'tamam al-mushtarak'
The passage says: 'If it is the complete common part between something and something else, then it is the genus.' This means genus is the entire shared essence, not a partial commonality. If the commonality is partial, then it is the differentia (fasl).
Differentia is the remaining part after removing the genus. For example, 'rationality' is the differentia for humans; it is not the full common part between humans and animals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف الجنس وهو المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالحقائق في جواب ما هو؟، فإن كان الجواب عن الماهية وعن بعض المشاركات هو الجواب عنها وعن الكل، فقريب كالحيوان، وإلا فبعيد، كالجسم النامي. فإن كان تمام المشترك بين شيء منها وبين بعض آخر فهو الجنس، وإلا فهو الفصل. ويقال لهذه الثلاثة ذاتيات، أو خارجا عنها ويقال له: العرضي، فإما أن يختص بأفراد حقيقةٍ واحدةٍ أو لا يختص، فالأول هو الخاصة والثاني هو العرض العام. فهذا دليل انحصار الكليات في الخمسة. قوله: المقول: أي المحمول.
Definition of genus: It is that which is predicated of many different things in essence in answer to 'what is it?'. If the answer about the quiddity and about some of the participants is the same as the answer about it and about all, then it is close, like 'animal'. Otherwise it is distant, like 'growing body'. If it is the complete common part between something and something else, then it is the genus, otherwise it is the differentia. And these three are called essential (dhatiyyat), or outside them and it is called accidental ('aradi). Then either it is specific to individuals of a single essence or not specific. The first is the property (khassa), the second is the common accident ('arad 'amm). So this is proof of the enumeration of the universals into five. His statement: 'al-maqul' means the predicate.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage defines genus (jins) as a universal term predicated of many different kinds (different in essence) in answer to 'what is it?' (ma huwa). The answer given to such a question reveals either the genus or the species. If the answer for a given quiddity and for some other kinds sharing a commonality is the same as the answer for that quiddity and for all kinds sharing that commonality, then the genus is close (qarib), like 'animal' for humans and horses. If not, it is distant (ba'id), like 'growing body' (jism nam) which is a wider category. The passage then distinguishes genus from differentia (fasl): if the common part shared between two things is complete (i.e., no further shared part beyond it), it is the genus; otherwise, it is the differentia. The three (genus, species, differentia) are called essential (dhatiyyat) because they are intrinsic. Outside these are accidents, which are either specific to one essence (property, khassa) or common to many (common accident, 'arad 'amm). Thus the five universals are exhaustively enumerated. The commentator notes that 'al-maqul' (the predicated) means the predicate (mahmul). The Islamic example here uses 'animal' (used in biology but also in usul al-fiqh for qiyas) and 'growing body' (a philosophical term).

Key Terms
الجنس
المقول على كثيرين
تمام المشترك

Key Takeaway
Genus is a universal that tells what something is, and it is close if it applies uniformly across all its instances.

From Genus to the Five Universals
Outlines the five universals using the genus concept as a foundation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given universal as one of the five universals: genus, species, differentia, property, common accident.

Core Idea
The five universals are exhaustively divided into essential and accidental, with two subdivisions in each category.

Having understood genus, we now see how it fits into the complete system of universals. The passage enumerates exactly five, proving by division. We will trace this classification from the essential (dhatiyyat) to the accidental, and within the accidental to property and common accident.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three essentials
The passage states that the first three—genus, species, differentia—are called essential (dhatiyyat). They are intrinsic to the essence of the thing.
These are the building blocks of definition.
2
Recognize the accidental
Anything not among the three essentials is called accidental ('aradi). This includes property and common accident.
Accidentals are not part of the essence.
3
Subdivide the accidental
The passage gives a condition: if the accidental is specific to individuals of a single essence, it is property (khassa); if not specific, it is common accident ('arad 'amm).
For example, 'laughter' is property of humans; 'motion' is common accident.
4
Verify the enumeration
The passage concludes that this division covers all possibilities, hence there are only five universals.
This is a standard argument by exhaustive division.

Source Text

Species and Genus in Answering 'What is it?'
Defines species and contrasts with genus using 'ma huwa' examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between species and genus when answering 'ma huwa' questions, and determine which universal to apply based on the nature of the subject(s).

Core Idea
The same universal can be species for individuals but genus for subordinate species; context determines the answer.

In Islamic logic, the question 'ma huwa' (what is it?) seeks the complete essence. The answer must be a universal that captures the shared reality of the subject(s). This lesson unpacks when the answer is a species and when it is a genus, using classical examples from the human and animal kinds. Mastering this distinction refines your ability to formulate precise definitions—a key skill in kalam, fiqh, and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject(s) of the question
Determine whether the question is about a single individual, multiple individuals of the same kind, or multiple entities of different kinds. The passage uses 'Zayd' (one person), 'Zayd, 'Amr, Bakr' (three humans), and 'human, horse, donkey' (three different species).
2
Check if the subjects share one essence or multiple essences
If all subjects have the same essence (like all humans), the answer can be a species. If they have different essences (like human and horse), the answer must be a genus. The passage states: 'If the question combines multiple things... if they are agreeing in essence... genus occurs in the answer'? Actually it says if they are agreeing in essence, the question is about the common essence; that common essence is genus when the essences are different.
Careful: 'agreeing in essence' here means sharing a common essential part despite different specific realities.
3
Apply the rule from the passage
Species occurs in answer to a single personal thing or to multiple things agreeing in essence. Genus occurs when the question covers different essences that share a common element. The passage gives the rule: 'species is in answer to a question about a single personal thing, and about multiple things agreeing in essence.' Genus is for multiple things with different essences sharing a common genus.
The same term can be both: 'human' is a species for individuals, but 'animal' is a genus for human, horse, donkey.
4
Test with concrete examples
For Zayd: answer 'human' (species). For Zayd, 'Amr, Bakr: answer 'human' (species). For human, horse, donkey: answer 'animal' (genus). The passage explains each: the complete definition of human is 'rational animal', of horse is 'neighing animal', etc., and the common genus is 'animal'.
If the question is about a universal essence (like 'what is human?'), the answer is the complete definition (hadd tamm), which includes genus and differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف النوع الثاني: النوع وهو المقول على كثيرين متفقين بالحقائق في جواب "ما هو". فيقع النوع في الجواب إن كان المذكور أمراً شخصياً، أو الحد التام إن كان المذكور حقيقةً كلية، وإن جمع في السؤال بين أمور كان السؤال عن تمام الماهية المشتركة بين تلك الأمور، ثم تلك الأمور إن كانت متفقة الحقيقة كان السؤال عن تمام الحقيقة المشتركة بين تلك الحقائق المختلفة، وقد عرفت أن تمام الذاتي المشترك بين الحقائق المختلفة هو الجنس، فيقع الجنس في الجواب، فالجنس لابد له أن يقع جوابا عن الماهية وعن بعض الحقائق المختلفة المشاركة إياها في ذلك الجنس، فإن كان مع ذلك جوابا عن الماهية وعن كل واحدة من الماهيات المختلفة فيقع النوع: فالنوع في الجواب عن السؤال بأمر واحد شخصي، وعن السؤال بأمور متعددة متفقة الحقيقة. فإذا سئل عن زيد بـ: ما هو ؟ فالجواب الإنسان، وإذا سئل عن زيد وعمرو وبكر فالجواب: الإنسان. (إسماعيل) فيقع الجنس: فإذا سئل عن الإنسان والفرس والحمار بـ"ما هو؟ يقع الحيوان في الجواب؛ فإن حقيقة كل واحد منها مخالفة لحقيقة الآخر، فحقيقة الإنسان الحيوان الناطق وحقيقة الفرس الحيوان الصاهل وحقيقة الحمار الحيوان الناهق والحيوان الذي هو جنس لها تمام الحقيقة المشتركة بينهما.
Definition of the second: Species (al-naw'), and it is that which is predicated of many agreeing in essence in answer to 'what is it?'. So species occurs in the answer if what is mentioned is a personal [individual] thing, or the complete definition if what is mentioned is a universal essence. And if the question combines multiple things, then the question is about the complete quiddity common among those things. Then if those things are agreeing in essence, the question is about the complete essence common among those different essences, and you have known that the complete essential common among different essences is the genus, so genus occurs in the answer. Thus genus must be an answer about the quiddity and about some of the different essences sharing that genus. Then if it is also an answer about the quiddity and about every one of the different quiddities, then species occurs. So species is in answer to a question about a single personal thing, and about multiple things agreeing in essence. For example, if one asks about Zayd: 'What is he?' the answer is 'human'. If one asks about Zayd, 'Amr, and Bakr, the answer is 'human' (Isma'il). Genus occurs: if one asks about human, horse, and donkey: 'What is it?' the answer is 'animal'; for the reality of each is different from the reality of the other: the reality of human is rational animal, the reality of horse is neighing animal, the reality of donkey is braying animal, and the animal which is their genus is the complete common essence among them.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage defines species (naw') as a universal predicated of many individuals that share the same essence, in answer to 'what is it?'. The species is the answer when the question is about a single individual (like Zayd) or about multiple individuals of the same kind (like Zayd, 'Amr, Bakr). The answer 'human' covers all of them. In contrast, when the question is about different kinds (like human, horse, donkey), the answer is the genus 'animal', which is the complete common essential part. The commentator Isma'il clarifies with examples: asking about individuals yields species; asking about diverse species yields genus. The passage also notes that if the question is about a universal essence (like the quiddity of human), the answer is the complete definition (hadd tamm), which includes genus and differentia. The key distinction is based on whether the things asked about share one essence or multiple essences. The example uses Zayd, 'Amr, Bakr (common names for humans) and horse/donkey (animals) – all from the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Key Terms
النوع
ما هو
الحد التام
الجنس

If a scholar asks 'What is Zayd?' and the answer is 'human', what kind of universal is 'human' in this context?
A) Genus, because it applies to many individuals.
B) Species, because it applies to many agreeing in essence.
C) Differentia, because it distinguishes Zayd from others.
D) Common accident, because it is accidental to Zayd.

Key Takeaway
Species answers 'what is it?' for individuals or multiple entities of the same essence, while genus answers for entities of different essences that share a common essential part.

Real and Relative Species and Genera Proximity
Defines real and relative species, explains proximity and distance of genera with a calculation rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real and relative species and calculate the degree of distance of a genus from its species using the rule of subtracting one from the number of answers.

Core Idea
A species can be real or relative; genera have measured distances that can be calculated.

This passage from Tahzeeb al-Mantiq clarifies two important distinctions within the five universals: the difference between real and relative species, and the measurement of how close or far a genus is from its species. These concepts are essential for accurate definition in Islamic logic and for constructing valid arguments in disciplines like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms being defined
Locate the definitions of 'real species' (al-nawʿ al-ḥaqīqī) and 'relative species' (al-nawʿ al-iḍāfī) in the Arabic. Note that real species is predicated on many individuals differing in quiddity, while relative species is any quiddity upon which and upon another thing a genus is predicated in answer to 'what is it?'.
2
Understand the overlap between them
Observe that the same entity (e.g., human) can be both a real species (since it is a universal predicated on individuals like Zayd and Amr) and a relative species (since it shares the genus 'animal' with other species). The text says 'between them there is generality and specificity in some respect'.
This overlap is why careful definition is needed.
3
Recognize close vs. distant genera
See that 'animal' is a close genus for human because it directly answers 'what is it?' for human and all its co-species. In contrast, 'body' is distant because it requires intermediate genera (e.g., growing body) to answer the question.
The text provides examples: human, horse, donkey.
4
Apply the rule for degrees of distance
The rule: subtract one from the number of distinct answers (jāwābāt) given for the participants in that genus. For human, 'growing body' yields two answers (growing body, animal) → one degree distant; 'body' yields three answers → two degrees; 'substance' yields four answers → three degrees.
Practice with other examples to internalize the rule.
5
Examine the defense of the definition
The passage defends the definition of relative species against the objection that it also applies to individuals (like Zayd) and sub-species (like Roman). The commentary explains that such cases are excluded because they are not quiddities predicated universally in answer to 'what is it?'.
This shows the importance of precision in logical definitions.

Source Text

Real and relative species. And it [relative species] may be said of the quiddity [mahiyyah] upon which and upon other than it is predicated the genus in answer to 'what is it?' and it is specifically named 'relative', like the first [species] is named 'real'. Between them there is generality and specificity in some respect; because they both apply to [the example of] human. [Something] participates with it [the quiddity] in that genus, so the genus is 'close' like animal when it occurs as an answer to the question about human and about everything that shares with him in animal quiddity, even if it does not occur as an answer about the quiddity? And everything that participates with it in that genus, so it is 'distant', like body when it occurs as an answer to the question about human and stone, and does not occur as an answer to the question about human and tree and horse for example. His statement: 'the quiddity upon which and upon other than it the genus is predicated' means the quiddity that is predicated in answer to 'what is it?'. Thus it is only a essential universal for what is under it, not a particular nor an accident. And all the participants in it, but it is not an answer about them and about each one of its participants individually. So if we are asked about human, horse, and donkey, the answer is not the growing body but rather animal, for it is the full commonality between them. (Isma'il). And it does not occur as an answer, for absolute body is not the full common reality between them, but the full commonality is the growing body, which is a part of it, so it is a part of the full commonality, and 'what is it?' demands the full commonality. Know that 'human' is a species, 'animal' is a close genus for it, 'growing body' is a distant genus by one degree, 'absolute body' is a distant genus by two degrees, and 'substance' is a distant genus by three degrees. If you wish to fix the degrees of distance, its rule is: subtract one from the number of answers occurring about its participants in that genus, and the remainder is the number of degrees of distance. So there are two answers if it is distant by one degree, like growing body with respect to human; three answers if it is distant by two degrees, like body; four answers if it is distant by three degrees, like substance; and so on. (Isma'il, with addition from al-Shamsiyya). 'The quiddity' means that what is intended by 'quiddity' in the definition of relative species is not absolute but rather what is predicated in answer to 'what is it?'. The purpose of this is to repel the objection that the definition of relative species as 'the quiddity predicated etc.' is not exclusive, because it applies to the individual and the sub-species; for the individual is also a quiddity upon which and upon other than it the genus is predicated in answer to 'what is it?', for if asked about Zayd and a horse 'what is it?' the answer is animal. Similarly the sub-species, which is the species restricted by an accidental restriction, like Roman and Abyssinian; for if asked about the Roman and the horse 'what is it?' the answer is animal. And he did not address the exclusion of the sub-species by restricting the predication to 'primarily', because that would exclude the lower species relative to the higher genera. As in the glosses of Jalal al-'Ulama' for him. (Abd).
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage defines two types of species: real (haqiqi) and relative (idafi). A real species is a universal predicated essentially on many individuals differing in quiddity (e.g., 'human' on Zayd, Amr). A relative species is any quiddity upon which and upon another thing a genus is predicated in answer to 'what is it?'. For example, 'human' is a real species but also a relative species because 'animal' is predicated on both human and horse. The passage then explains that genera can be close (qarib) or distant (ba'id). A close genus is one that answers 'what is it?' for all its species without needing an intermediate genus, like 'animal' for human. A distant genus requires intermediate genera, like 'body' for human (through 'growing body'). The author provides a rule to calculate the degree of distance: subtract one from the number of distinct answers for the participants in that genus. For example, for human, 'growing body' yields two answers (growing body, animal), so one degree distant; 'body' yields three (body, growing body, animal), so two degrees; 'substance' yields four, so three degrees. The commentary then defends the definition of relative species by noting that it excludes individuals (like Zayd) and sub-species (like Roman) because they are not quiddities predicated in answer to 'what is it?' in the same way—they are either particular or accidental restrictions. This ensures the definition is exclusive.

النوع الإضافي
الجنس القريب
الجنس البعيد

Real and Relative Species: Relationship and Simple Species
Explains the relationship of general and special (عموم من وجه) between real and relative species, including the simple species (point) as a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish real from relative species and identify whether a simple species is both or just one, based on the later scholars' criterion of 'عموم من وجه'.

Core Idea
A simple species like the point or intellect is real but not relative—no genus means no relativity.

In this passage, we explore the nuanced relation between real and relative species. Early scholars saw absolute inclusion; later scholars saw partial overlap. The key test is whether a species can also be a genus or be under a genus—the simple species breaks the pattern. Let's deconstruct this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions of real and relative species
Real species: predicated on many in answer to 'what is it?'. Relative species: is itself predicated on other species as a genus, or is subsumed under a higher genus.
2
Map the relations: full inclusion vs partial overlap
Early view: all relative are either real or genus; real are always relative because under a genus (except simple). Later view: some real species are not relative (simple species). This changes the relationship to partial overlap.
3
Examine the objection about sub-species (صنف)
The text objects: if sub-species is a species, why exclude it from the five? Answer: it is a non-inclusive property (خاصة غير شاملة) - composite of essential and accidental.
4
Analyze the simple species example: point (نقطة)
The point is a real species because it is a quiddity, but has no genus or differentia, so it is simple and cannot be relative. This illustrates the later view.
The example shows that not all real species are relative; thus the relationship is 'عموم من وجه'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالشخص كزيد والصنف كالرومي ،مثلا خارجان عنها. فالنوع الإضافي دائمًا إما أن يكون نوعا حقيقيا مندرجا تحت جنس كالإنسان تحت الحيوان وإما جنسا مندرجا تحت جنس آخر، كالحيوان تحت الجسم النامي، ففي الأول يتصادق النوع الحقيقي والإضافي، وفي الثاني يوجد الإضافي بدون الحقيقي، ويجوز أيضا تحقق الحقيقي بدون الإضافي فيما إذا كان النوع بسيطا لا جزء له حتى يكون جنسا، وقد مثل بالنقطة، عنها: أي عن تلك الماهية، وقد يشكل بأن الصنف إما نوع أو خاصة أو لا هذا ولا ذاك، فإن كان الأول فلا وجه لإخراجه، وإن كان الثاني فلا وجه في إخراجه إلى اعتبار قيد آخر؛ لأن قوله : " في جواب ما هو "؟ يخرج الخاصة، وإن كان الثالث فيلزم أن لا يكون الكليات منحصرة في الخمسة؛ لأن الصنف ليس بجنس ولا فصل ولا عرض عام بالضرورة، فإذا لم يكن نوعا وخاصة يكون أمرا آخر سوى الخمس، والجواب: أن الصنف خاصة غير شاملة؛ لأنه عبارة عن الماهية النوعية مع بعض الصفات والمركب من الذاتي والعرضي عرض. (عبد) فالنوع الإضافي: شروع في بيان النسبة بين النوع الإضافي والنوع الحقيقي. اعلم أن القدماء ذهبوا إلى أن النسبة بينهما عموم وخصوص مطلقا؛ فإن الإنسان نوع حقيقي؛ لكونه مقولا على كثيرين متفقين بالحقائق، ونوع إضافي أيضا من حيث أنه يقال عليه وعلى الفرس الجنس أي الحيوان فهذا مادة التصادق، وأما مادة التفارق فهو الحيوان؛ فإنه ليس نوعا حقيقيا مع كونه إضافيا حيث يقال عليها وعلى النباتات الجنس أي الجسم النامي وأما المتأخرون فذهبوا إلى أن النسبة بينهما عموم وخصوص من ،وجه وقالوا يمكن أن يتحقق الحقيقي بدون الإضافي فيما إذا كان النوع بسيطا كالعقل، والحق هو هذا المذهب كما سيأتي تحقيقه، ولذا اختاره المصنف قدس سره. ( إسماعيل) مندرجا تحت جنس أو لم يكن مندرجا تحت جنس كما أشار إليه بقوله: ويجوز أيضا تحقق الحقيقي بدون الإضافي إلخ فلا يرد أن بين قوله: "دائما يكون نوعا حقيقيا إلخ وبين قوله: ويجوز أيضا تحقق الحقيقي بدون الإضافي" منافاة كما لا يخفى. فتأمل. 11 بالنقطة فإنه نوع حقيقي وليس بنوع ،إضافي، وإلا لكان مركبا؛ لوجوب اندراج النوع الإضافي تحت جنس، وما له جنس يكون له فصل بالضرورة، فيكون مركبا من الجنس والفصل. تحرير القواعد المنطقية بزيادة)
For the individual like Zayd and the sub-species like Roman, for example, are excluded from it [the relative species definition]. So the relative species is always either a real species subsumed under a genus, like human under animal, or a genus subsumed under another genus, like animal under growing body. In the first case, the real and relative species coincide; in the second, the relative exists without the real. And it is also possible for the real to exist without the relative when the species is simple, having no part such that it could be a genus, and it has been exemplified by the point. 'Anha' (from it) meaning from that quiddity. And it may be objected that the sub-species is either a species, or a property, or neither. If the first, there is no reason to exclude it; if the second, there is no reason to exclude it by considering another restriction because his statement 'in answer to what is it?' excludes the property; if the third, it would necessitate that the universals are not confined to the five, because the sub-species is necessarily neither a genus, nor a differentia, nor a common accident. So if it is not a species and a property, it would be something other than the five. The answer: the sub-species is a non-inclusive property, because it is the specific quiddity with some attributes, and the composite of essential and accidental is accidental. (Abd) 'So the relative species' is a start in explaining the relation between the relative and real species. Know that the early scholars held that the relation between them is absolute generality and specificity; for human is a real species because it is predicated on many agreeing in quiddity, and also a relative species from the perspective that the genus (animal) is predicated on it and on the horse. So this is the area of agreement. As for the area of difference, it is animal, for it is not a real species while being relative, because the genus (growing body) is predicated on it and on plants. The later scholars held that the relation between them is generality and specificity in some respect, and they said that it is possible for the real to exist without the relative when the species is simple like the intellect. The truth is this doctrine, as its verification will come, and therefore the author (may his secret be sanctified) chose it. (Isma'il) Whether it is subsumed under a genus or not subsumed under a genus, as he indicated by his statement: 'And it is also possible for the real to exist without the relative etc.', so there is no contradiction, as is not hidden. So reflect. 11 With the point, for it is a real species and not a relative species, otherwise it would be composite, because the relative species must be subsumed under a genus, and whatever has a genus necessarily has a differentia, so it would be composite of genus and differentia. (Tahrir al-Qawa'id al-Mantiqiyya with additions)
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on real and relative species, focusing on their relationship and the case of a simple species. It begins by reaffirming that individuals (like Zayd) and sub-species (like Roman) are excluded from the definition of relative species. The relative species is always either a real species under a genus (like human under animal) or a genus under another genus (like animal under growing body). In the first scenario, real and relative coincide; in the second, relative exists without real. Then the passage introduces the possibility of a real species existing without being relative, exemplified by a simple species like the point. A simple species has no parts (no genus and differentia), so it cannot be a relative species because relative species require subsumption under a higher genus. The passage then addresses an objection about sub-species: if a sub-species is a species, why exclude it? The answer: the sub-species is a non-inclusive property (khassa ghayr shamila), i.e., an accidental composite, so it is not a quiddity answering 'what is it?' in the required way. The commentary then discusses the differing views of early and later scholars on the relation between real and relative species. Early scholars (al-mutaqaddimun) held that the relation is absolute generality and specificity (عموم وخصوص مطلق): all relative species are not necessarily real (e.g., animal is relative but not real), and real species are all relative (since they are under a genus). Later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirun) held it is generality and specificity in some respect (عموم وخصوص من وجه), allowing a real species that is not relative, like the simple intellect. The author supports this later view. The point illustrates that a simple real species exists without being relative and without composition.

Key Terms
عموم وخصوص من وجه
النوع البسيط
الصنف
