The Five Universals
intermediate
10 min
Defining the Differentia: The Problem of 'Ayy' and al-Tusi's Solution
Author defines the differentia and resolves issues with 'ayy'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logicians restrict the word 'ayy' to the differentia and apply al-Tusi's method to identify the differentia of a species.

Core Idea
The differentia is not asked about until the genus is already known.

This lesson tackles a central problem in defining the differentia: the word 'ayy' (which) seems to apply to the complete definition and genus as well. The author presents a technical restriction by logicians and a more precise method by al-Tusi. You will learn to navigate this problem and correctly identify the differentia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem with 'ayy'
The word 'ayy' in the definition of differentia causes confusion because it can also be asked for the complete definition and genus. Locate where the author mentions that the people of intellect have stipulated that 'ayy' is restricted to a distinguisher not said in answer to 'what is it?'.
This is the first solution mentioned: technical restriction.
2
Analyze al-Tusi's solution
Al-Tusi says we only ask for the differentia after knowing the genus. For example, for 'man' we ask: 'which animal is he in his essence?' Since 'animal' is already known as the genus, the answer must be the differentia 'rational'.
This method is more precise because it presumes genus knowledge.
3
Understand the kinaya of 'thing'
The word 'thing' (shay') in the definition of differentia is a kinaya for the known genus. Logicians used this because enumerating all genera is impossible. So 'thing' stands for whatever genus is relevant.
Kinaya avoids the need to list every possible genus.
4
Apply the method to a new example
Given the species 'horse' with genus 'animal', ask: 'Horse, which animal is it in its essence?' The genus is known, so answer with its differentia (e.g., 'neighing' or another characteristic).
Practice ensures mastery of the method.

في هذا المقام، وأجاب صاحب المحاكمات بأن معنى "أي" وإن كان بحسب اللغة طلب المميز مطلقا لكن أرباب المعقول اصطلحوا على أنه يطلب به مميز لا يكون مقولا في جواب ما هو، وبهذا يخرج الحد والجنس أيضًا، وللمحقق الطوسي ههنا مسلك آخر أدق وأتقن وهو إنا لا نسأل عن الفصل إلا بعد أن نعلم أن للشيء جنسا؛ بناء على أن ما لا جنس له لا فصل له، وإذا علمنا الشيء بالجنس فنطلب ما يميزه عن مشاركاته في ذلك الجنس، فنقول: الإنسان أي حيوان هو في ذاته؟ فتعين الجواب بالناطق لا غير. فكلمته "شيء" صاحب المحاكمات يعني مولانا قطب الدين الرازي يطلب به حاصل الجواب: اختيار الشق الثاني يعني أن المراد ما يميز تميزا في الجملة لكن لا مطلقا بل المراد المميز الذي لا يكون مقولا في جواب ما هو الحد التام ليس بمقول في جواب ما هو إلا إذا سئل عن الأمر الكلي والجنس إذا سئل عن مختلفة الحقائق والتمييز كل واحد منهما في الجملة يقالان في جواب ما هو ما يقال في جواب ما هو لا يقال في جواب أي شيء هو في ذاته بحسب اصطلاح أرباب العقول وصحة وقوعها في جواب أي شيء بحسب اللغة لا يضرنا؛ لأن كلامنا في مصطلحهم. (عبد) آخر في دفع الإشكال المذكور. (تحفه) أدق لأن فيه ملاحظة معنى الفصل وحال السائل الطالب به بأنه علم الجنس أولا ثم يطلب فصلا. وأتقن السلامة عن الطعن الذي في جواب العلامة الرازي لله، وهو أن الجواب بأن أرباب المعقول اصطلحوا هكذا ولا مشاحة في الاصطلاح جواب على رسم أرباب المعقول. (عبد) بالناطق لأن الجنس قد علم فلا حاجة إلى الجواب به فقط ولا بانضمامه إلى الناطق. فإن قلت: إن الناطق بمعنى مدرك الكليات ليس مختصا بالإنسان؛ لأن الباري عز شأنه وسائر المجردات كالعقول والنفوس الفلكية يدركون الكليات أيضاً، فلا يصح كونه فصلا قريبا للإنسان؛ لما تقرر عندهم أن الفصل القريب لابد أن يكون مختصاً بالماهية التي هو فصل لها، ولو سلم أن الفصل القريب لا يجب أن يكون مختصا بتلك الماهية فجعله فصلا للإنسان ليس أولى من جعله جنسا له بل يجوز أن يجعل الحيوان فصلا أو الناطق جنسا؛ فإن كل واحد منهما مشترك بين ماهية الإنسان وغيرها قلت المراد من الناطق هو صاحب مبدأ النطق والإدراك، ولا شك أن ذلك المبدأ مختص بماهية الإنسان الذي هو أثر ذلك المبدأ فلا إشكال. وقد أجاب بعض القاصرين بأن المراد بالناطق : = أقسام الفصل  القريب فقريب وإلّا ،فبعيد، وإذا نسب إلى ما يميزه فمقوّم، وإلى ما يميز عنه فمقسم، في التعريف كناية عن الجنس المعلوم الذي يطلب ما يميز الشيء عن المشاركات في ذلك ،الجنس، فحينئذ يندفع الإشكال بحذافيره قوله فقريب كالناطق بالنسبة إلى الإنسان؛ حيث يميزه عن المشاركات في جنسه القريب وهو الحيوان. :قوله فبعيد كالحساس بالنسبة إلى الإنسان؛ حيث يميزه عن المشاركات في الجنس البعيد وهو الجسم النامي. قوله: إذا نسب إلخ: الفصل له نسبة إلى الماهية التي هو مخصص ومميز لها ونسبة إلى الجنس الذي يميز الماهية عنه من بين أفراده، فهو بالاعتبار الأول = في تعريف الإنسان هو المتكلم بالحروف والصوت وأنه مخصوص بالإنسان؛ لأن المتكلم ثلاثة المتكلم بلا صوت وحرف كالباري عز شأنه وجل ،برهانه والمتكلم بما ينتزع عنه الحروف والصوت كسائر الحيوانات، والمتكلم بالحروف والصوت وهو الإنسان وأما الملائكة التي هي العقول المجردة عندهم والنفوس الفلكية ليس لها تكلم ونطق أصلا، ولا يخفى على أولي الألباب أن هذا الجواب ينبغي أن يطرح تحت الميزاب. (عبد) كناية وإنما اختاروا الكناية؛ لتعذر حصر الأجناس وذكرها في تعريف الفصل، فوضعوا لفظ شيء موضعها كناية عما يشمل الأجناس. (تحفه) كذلك كالناطق: الذي هو صاحب مبدأ النطق والإدراك، ولا شك أنه مختص بماهية الإنسان. كالحساس: ههنا إشكال وهو أن الحساس كما أنه مميز للإنسان عن مشاركاته في الجنس البعيد وهو الجسم النامي كذلك الناطق أيضاً مميز له؛ فإن الحساس كما يميز الإنسان عن النباتات بالمشاركة في الجسم النامي كذلك الناطق أيضاً مميز له؛ فإن الحساس كما يميز الإنسان عن النباتات بالمشاركة في الجسم النامي الناطق أيضاً يميزه عنها؛ فإن الناطق فصل قريب وقد صدق عليه تعريف الفصل البعيد فلم يكن مانعا. ويمكن الجواب عنه بأن قيد "فقط" مراد بعد قول المصنف والبعيد ، فحاصل تعريف الفصل ما يميز عن المشاركات في الجنس البعيد فقط، والفصل القريب وإن كان مميزا عن المشاركات في الجنس البعيد إلا أنه مميز عن المشاركات في الجنس القريب أيضاً. فافهم.(إسماعيل)
The mental concepts. V. Definition of the differentia. On this topic, the author of al-Muhakamat replied that the meaning of 'which' (ayy), although linguistically is an unrestricted request for a distinguisher, the people of intellect have stipulated that by it is sought a distinguisher that is not said in answer to 'what is it?', and by this the definition and genus are also excluded. And al-Muhaqqiq al-Tusi has here another path, more precise and more accurate, which is that we do not ask about the differentia unless after we know that the thing has a genus, based on the principle that what has no genus has no differentia. And when we know the thing by its genus, we then ask for what distinguishes it from its associates in that genus, so we say: 'Man, which animal is he in his essence?' So the answer is determined to be 'rational' and nothing else. So his word 'thing' (shay') [is a kinaya]. The author of al-Muhakamat, meaning our master Qutb al-Din al-Razi, seeks by it the result of the answer: choosing the second part, meaning that what is intended is that which distinguishes generally, but not absolutely; rather what is intended is the distinguisher that is not said in answer to 'what is it?' The complete definition is not said in answer to 'what is it?' except when asked about a universal matter; and the genus, when asked about different realities, and the differentiation, each one of them generally, are said in answer to 'what is it?' That which is said in answer to 'what is it?' is not said in answer to 'which thing is it in its essence?' according to the terminology of the people of intellect. And the correctness of their occurrence in answer to 'which thing?' linguistically does not harm us, because our discourse concerns their terminology. (Abd) Another in repelling the mentioned difficulty. (Tuhfa) More precise because it includes consideration of the meaning of the differentia and the state of the questioner seeking it by it, that he first knows the genus then seeks a differentia. And more accurate because of safety from the criticism found in the answer of 'Allama al-Razi—God [have mercy on him]—namely that the answer that the people of intellect have stipulated thus and there is no quarrel over terminology is an answer according to the custom of the people of intellect. (Abd) By 'rational' because the genus is already known, so there is no need to answer with it alone nor with its combination with 'rational'. If you say: 'Rational' in the sense of perceiver of universals is not specific to man, because God—mighty is His majesty—and other incorporeal beings like the intellects and celestial souls also perceive universals, so it is not valid for it to be a proximal differentia for man, because it is established among them that the proximal differentia must be specific to the essence for which it is a differentia. And even if one concedes that the proximal differentia need not be specific to that essence, then making it a differentia for man is no more appropriate than making it a genus for him; rather it is permitted to make 'animal' a differentia or 'rational' a genus, for each shares between the essence of man and others. I say: What is meant by 'rational' is the possessor of the principle of speech and perception, and there is no doubt that that principle is specific to the essence of man, which is the effect of that principle, so there is no difficulty. And some of the deficient ones have replied that what is meant by 'rational' is... Divisions of the differentia. The proximal is proximal, otherwise it is distal. And when it is related to what it distinguishes, it is constitutive, and to which it distinguishes from, it is divisive. In the definition it is a kinaya for the known genus that seeks what distinguishes the thing from its associates in that genus, so then the difficulty is repelled completely. He says: 'Proximal' like 'rational' in relation to man, where it distinguishes him from his associates in his proximal genus, which is animal. He says: 'Distal' like 'sensitive' in relation to man, where it distinguishes him from his associates in the distal genus, which is the growing body. He says: 'When it is related, etc.' The differentia has a relation to the essence for which it is a particularizer and distinguisher, and a relation to the genus from which it distinguishes the essence among its individuals. So by the first consideration... In defining man, he is the speaker with letters and sound, and that he is specific to man; because the speaker is three: the speaker without sound and letter like God—mighty is His majesty and glorious is His proof—and the speaker by what is abstracted from letters and sound like other animals, and the speaker with letters and sound—and this is man. As for the angels, which are the pure intellects according to them, and the celestial souls, they have no speech and utterance at all. And it is not hidden from people of intellect that this answer should be thrown under the drain. (Abd) Kinaya: and they chose the kinaya because of the impossibility of enumerating the genera and mentioning them in the definition of the differentia; so they placed the word 'thing' in place of them as a kinaya for what encompasses the genera. (Tuhfa) Likewise, like 'rational': which is the possessor of the principle of speech and perception, and there is no doubt that it is specific to the essence of man. Like 'sensitive': Here there is a difficulty: that 'sensitive' as it distinguishes man from his associates in the distal genus, which is the growing body, likewise 'rational' also distinguishes him; for 'sensitive' as it distinguishes man from plants by participation in the growing body, likewise 'rational' also distinguishes him from them; for 'rational' is a proximal differentia and the definition of the distal differentia applies to it, so it is not a hindrance. And it is possible to answer this by saying that the condition 'only' (faqat) is intended after the author's statement 'and the distal', so the definition of the differentia is that which distinguishes from associates in the distal genus only, and the proximal differentia, although it distinguishes from associates in the distal genus, nonetheless it distinguishes from associates in the proximal genus as well. So understand. (Isma'il)
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage defines the five universals (specifically the differentia) and discusses its divisions. The author begins by addressing a problem: the word 'which' (ayy) in the definition of differentia seems to apply to the complete definition and genus as well. The author of al-Muhakamat responds that logicians have restricted 'which' to the differentia, so it is a technical term. Al-Tusi offers a more precise path: we only ask about the differentia after knowing the genus. So when we ask 'man, which animal is he?', we already know his genus, and the answer must be the differentia (rational). The word 'thing' (shay') in the definition of differentia is a kinaya for the known genus. This kinaya is explained by 'Abd as a device to avoid enumerating all genera. The passage then classifies differentia: proximal vs. distal (e.g., rational vs. sensitive for man), and constitutive vs. divisive. Two objections are raised and answered: (1) that 'rational' is not exclusive to man because God and angels perceive universals; the response redefines 'rational' as the possessor of the principle of speech and perception, which is unique to humans, or alternatively that man is the speaker with letters and sound. This second response is criticized as weak. (2) That 'sensitive' as a distal differentia also applies to rational, but the answer stipulates that the distal differentia distinguishes only from the distal genus, while the proximal differentia also distinguishes from the proximal genus. All examples used are from the Islamic sciences: man, animals, plants, God, angels, etc.

Checkpoint
According to al-Tusi, why is the answer 'rational' when asked 'which animal is man?'
A) Because the questioner does not know the genus
B) Because the genus is already known
C) Because 'rational' is the only word that answers 'which'
D) Because 'animal' is not a differentia

Key Takeaway
The differentia is the distinguisher sought after the genus is known, and the word 'ayy' is technically restricted to that role in logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the species 'horse', with genus 'animal', formulate the correct differentia question in Arabic according to al-Tusi's method and provide the expected answer.
?
Explain why the complete definition 'rational animal' cannot be the answer to the 'which' question in the technical sense, using the distinction between 'what is it?' and 'which is it?'.

The Five Universals
intermediate
8 min
Divisions of the Differentia: Proximal, Distal, Constitutive, and Divisive
Author classifies the differentia into proximal/distal and constitutive/divisive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given differentia as proximal or distal based on the genus it distinguishes from, and as constitutive or divisive based on its relation to the essence or genus.

Core Idea
The differentia is defined by its proximity to the genus and its role in constituting or dividing the essence.

After defining the differentia, the author explains its divisions: proximal (qarib) vs. distal (ba'id), and constitutive (muqawwim) vs. divisive (muqassim). Using man's differentiae 'rational' and 'sensitive', you will learn to correctly categorize any differentia according to these dimensions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two classification criteria
The differentia is divided by nearness to the genus (proximal vs. distal) and by its relation to the essence versus the genus (constitutive vs. divisive). These are two distinct perspectives.
Proximal differentia distinguishes from the immediate genus; distal from a higher genus.
2
Understand proximal vs. distal
For man, 'rational' is proximal because it distinguishes from the proximal genus 'animal'. 'Sensitive' is distal because it distinguishes from the distant genus 'growing body' (jism nabit).
Distal differentiae are broader but less specific.
3
Understand constitutive vs. divisive
When the differentia relates to the essence (i.e., it determines the species), it is constitutive (muqawwim). When it relates to the genus (i.e., it divides the genus into species), it is divisive (muqassim). Example: 'rational' is constitutive for man, and also divisive for animal.
A differentia can be both constitutive and divisive depending on the perspective.
4
Apply to a new example
Take the species 'horse'. If its proximal differentia is 'neighing' (al-sahil), classify it: proximal (distinguishing from animal), constitutive (for horse), divisive (for animal). Then consider a distal differentia like 'sensitive' for horse.
Distal differentiae may apply to many species; check the genus.

تعريف الفصل
في هذا المقام، وأجاب صاحب المحاكمات بأن معنى "أي" وإن كان بحسب اللغة طلب المميز مطلقا لكن أرباب المعقول اصطلحوا على أنه يطلب به مميز لا يكون مقولا في جواب ما هو، وبهذا يخرج الحد والجنس أيضًا، وللمحقق الطوسي ههنا مسلك آخر أدق وأتقن وهو إنا لا نسأل عن الفصل إلا بعد أن نعلم أن للشيء جنسا؛ بناء على أن ما لا جنس له لا فصل له، وإذا علمنا الشيء بالجنس فنطلب ما يميزه عن مشاركاته في ذلك الجنس، فنقول: الإنسان أي حيوان هو في ذاته؟ فتعين الجواب بالناطق لا غير. فكلمته "شيء" صاحب المحاكمات يعني مولانا قطب الدين الرازي يطلب به حاصل الجواب: اختيار الشق الثاني يعني أن المراد ما يميز تميزا في الجملة لكن لا مطلقا بل المراد المميز الذي لا يكون مقولا في جواب ما هو الحد التام ليس بمقول في جواب ما هو إلا إذا سئل عن الأمر الكلي والجنس إذا سئل عن مختلفة الحقائق والتمييز كل واحد منهما في الجملة يقالان في جواب ما هو ما يقال في جواب ما هو لا يقال في جواب أي شيء هو في ذاته بحسب اصطلاح أرباب العقول وصحة وقوعها في جواب أي شيء بحسب اللغة لا يضرنا؛ لأن كلامنا في مصطلحهم. (عبد) آخر في دفع الإشكال المذكور. (تحفه) أدق لأن فيه ملاحظة معنى الفصل وحال السائل الطالب به بأنه علم الجنس أولا ثم يطلب فصلا. وأتقن السلامة عن الطعن الذي في جواب العلامة الرازي لله، وهو أن الجواب بأن أرباب المعقول اصطلحوا هكذا ولا مشاحة في الاصطلاح جواب على رسم أرباب المعقول. In defining man, he is the speaker with letters and sound, and that he is specific to man; because the speaker is three: the speaker without sound and letter like God—mighty is His majesty and glorious is His proof—and the speaker by what is abstracted from letters and sound like other animals, and the speaker with letters and sound—and this is man. As for the angels, which are the pure intellects according to them, and the celestial souls, they have no speech and utterance at all. And it is not hidden from people of intellect that this answer should be thrown under the drain. (Abd) Kinaya: and they chose the kinaya because of the impossibility of enumerating the genera and mentioning them in the definition of the differentia; so they placed the word 'thing' in place of them as a kinaya for what encompasses the genera. (Tuhfa) Likewise, like 'rational': which is the possessor of the principle of speech and perception, and there is no doubt that it is specific to the essence of man. Like 'sensitive': Here there is a difficulty: that 'sensitive' as it distinguishes man from his associates in the distal genus, which is the growing body, likewise 'rational' also distinguishes him; for 'sensitive' as it distinguishes man from plants by participation in the growing body, likewise 'rational' also distinguishes him from them; for 'rational' is a proximal differentia and the definition of the distal differentia applies to it, so it is not a hindrance. And it is possible to answer this by saying that the condition 'only' (faqat) is intended after the author's statement 'and the distal', so the definition of the differentia is that which distinguishes from associates in the distal genus only, and the proximal differentia, although it distinguishes from associates in the distal genus, nonetheless it distinguishes from associates in the proximal genus as well. So understand. (Isma'il)
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage defines the five universals (specifically the differentia) and discusses its divisions. The author begins by addressing a problem: the word 'which' (ayy) in the definition of differentia seems to apply to the complete definition and genus as well. The author of al-Muhakamat responds that logicians have restricted 'which' to the differentia, so it is a technical term. Al-Tusi offers a more precise path: we only ask about the differentia after knowing the genus. So when we ask 'man, which animal is he?', we already know his genus, and the answer must be the differentia (rational). The word 'thing' (shay') in the definition of differentia is a kinaya for the known genus. This kinaya is explained by 'Abd as a device to avoid enumerating all genera. The passage then classifies differentia: proximal vs. distal (e.g., rational vs. sensitive for man), and constitutive vs. divisive. Two objections are raised and answered: (1) that 'rational' is not exclusive to man because God and angels perceive universals; the response redefines 'rational' as the possessor of the principle of speech and perception, which is unique to humans, or alternatively that man is the speaker with letters and sound. This second response is criticized as weak. (2) That 'sensitive' as a distal differentia also applies to rational, but the answer stipulates that the distal differentia distinguishes only from the distal genus, while the proximal differentia also distinguishes from the proximal genus. All examples used are from the Islamic sciences: man, animals, plants, God, angels, etc.

Key Terms

قريب
بعيد
مقوّم
مقسّم
كناية

Key Takeaway
The differentia has two dimensions: nearness to genus (proximal/distal) and relation to essence/genus (constitutive/divisive).

The Differentia as Constituent and Divider
Author defines the differentia in two respects: as constituent and as divider.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the differentia as constituent of essence and as divider of genus, and identify which role is being used in a given definition.

Core Idea
The same differentia both builds the species and divides the genus.

The differentia (fasl) plays a dual role in definition. It is not merely a distinguisher; it also constitutes the essence of the species and, at the same time, divides the genus into its species. Understanding this dual function is crucial for constructing and evaluating definitions in Islamic logic and sciences like usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus in the example
The passage uses 'animal' (hayawan) as the genus. Notice that the genus is the broader category that gets divided.
2
Identify the differentia
The differentia is 'rational' (natiq). It is the property that, when added to the genus, picks out a specific species (human).
3
See how the differentia constitutes the essence
The differentia is called muqawwim because it is a part of the mahiyya (essence). Without 'rational', the species 'human' would not be what it is; it is a constituent part.
4
See how the differentia divides the genus
The same differentia, when combined with the genus, splits the genus into two: rational animal (human) and non-rational animal (other animals). This is the muqassim role.
5
Apply to any definition
In any hadd (definition), ask: Does this differentia constitute the species? Does it divide the genus? Often it does both simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الفصل يسمى مقوما؛ لأنه جزء الماهية ومحصل لها، وبالاعتبار الثاني يسمى مقسما؛ لأنه بانضمامه إلى هذا الجنس وجودا يحصل قسما وعدما يحصل قسما آخر كما ترى في تقسيم الحيوان إلى الحيوان الناطق وإلى الحيوان الغير الناطق.
Divisions of the differentia. It is called a constituent because it is a part of the essence and an actualizer of it. By the second consideration it is called a divider because by its conjunction with this genus, existence-wise it produces a division, and non-existence-wise it produces another division, as you see in the division of animal into rational animal and non-rational animal.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage defines the two aspects of the differentia (fasl). First, it is called muqawwim (constituent) because it is a part of the essence (mahiyya) and actualizes it. Second, it is called muqassim (divider) because when it joins the genus, it divides it. The example given is the division of animal (hayawan) into rational and non-rational animal. The differentia 'rational' (natiq) when added to the genus 'animal' produces the species 'human'. This shows the differentia's dual role.

Key Terms
فصل (fasl): The differentia that distinguishes a species from other species under the same genus. Here it is analyzed as muqawwim (constituent of essence) and muqassim (divider of genus). [Note: In earlier texts, fasl is primarily a logical tool; later in usul al-fiqh it takes on legal implications.]
مقوم (muqawwim): That which constitutes the essence; the differentia as a part of the mahiyya.
مقسم (muqassim): That which divides the genus into species; the differentia as a principle of division.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It only constitutes the essence of human.
B) It only divides the genus animal.
C) It both constitutes the essence of human and divides the genus animal.
D) It is neither muqawwim nor muqassim.

Key Takeaway
The differentia serves a dual role: it is both a constituent of the species' essence and a principle that divides the genus into species.

The No-Converse Rule for Constituents and Dividers
Explains the asymmetry between constituents and dividers in genus hierarchy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which logical relationships hold between constituents and dividers of higher and lower genera, and explain why the converse does not apply in both directions.

Core Idea
Not every part of a lower is part of a higher, but every divider of a lower divides the higher.

This lesson examines the logical relationship between the constituents (muqawwim) and dividers (muqassim) of genera and species. You will learn why a differentia that constitutes a lower species is not necessarily a constituent of the higher genus, while a differentia that divides a lower species also divides the higher genus. Understanding this subtle distinction is crucial for constructing accurate definitions in Islamic logic and avoiding fallacious reasoning in theological and jurisprudential debates.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish 'constituent' (muqawwim) and 'divider' (muqassim)
A constituent is an essential part of the definition (e.g., 'rational' for man), while a divider is a differentia that splits a genus into species (e.g., 'sensitive' dividing body into animal and plant).
The passage treats both as differentiae but with different logical behaviors.
2
Identify the higher and lower terms in the example
The passage uses 'man' (lower) and 'animal' (higher). Note that higher/lower include intermediate levels: a genus can be both higher and lower relative to different terms.
For instance, 'intermediate genus' is higher than what is below it and lower than what is above it.
3
Apply the no-converse rule for constituents
Not every constituent of the lower (e.g., 'rational') is a constituent of the higher (animal). Rational does not define animal. The passage clarifies that 'no converse' here negates the universal affirmative, not the universal negative.
The objection that the converse (some constituent of lower is constituent of higher) is true is addressed: it is true but does not contradict the rule.
4
Apply the no-converse rule for dividers
Every divider of the lower is a divider of the higher (since the lower is a division of the higher), but not conversely. For example, 'sensitive' divides the higher (growing body) but not the lower (animal), because animal is already defined by sensation.
Thus, the divider rule is the opposite of the constituent rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ولا عكس أي كليا بمعنى أنه ليس كل مقوم للسافل مقوما للعالي؛ فإن الناطق مقوم للسافل الذي هو الإنسان، وليس مقوما للعالي الذي هو الحيوان. وليعلم أن المراد بالعالي ههنا كل جنس أو نوع يكون فوق آخر سواء كان فوقه آخر أو لم يكن، وكذا المراد بالسافل كل جنس أو نوع يكون تحت آخر سواء كان تحته آخر أو لم يكن، حتى أن الجنس المتوسط عال بالنسبة إلى ما تحته وسافل بالنسبة إلى ما فوقه. كليا دفع دخل هو: أنه كل مقوم للعالي مقوم للسافل قضية موجبة كلية وعكسها بعض مقوم للسافل مقوم للعالي موجبة جزئية صادق بالضرورة، فلا معنى لقوله لا عكس فتوضيح الدفع أن قول الشارح كليا إيماء إلى أن قوله لا عكس رفع الإيجاب الكلي لا السلب الكلي ورفع الإيجاب الكلي لا ينافيه الإيجاب الجزئي. كل مقوم فصلا قريبا أو بعيدا فلا يرد أنه إن أريد بالمقوم الفصل القريب فلا شيء من المقوم القريب للسافل مقوم للعالي، وإن أريد الفصل البعيد فكل مقوم بعيد للسافل مقوم للعالي. والمقسم بالعكس أي كل مقسم للسافل مقسم للعالي ولا عكس أي كليا. أما الأول؛ فلأن السافل قسم من العالي، فكل فصل حصل للسافل قسما فقد حصل للعالي قسما؛ لأن قسم القسم قسم. وأما الثاني؛ فلأن الحساس مثلا مقسم للعالي الذي هو الجسم النامي وليس مقسما للسافل الذي هو الحيوان.
His statement: 'and no converse' meaning that it is a universal negative, in the sense that not every constituent of the lower is a constituent of the higher; for example, 'rational' is a constituent of the lower which is man, but it is not a constituent of the higher which is animal. And let it be known that by 'higher' here is meant every genus or species that is above another, whether or not there is something above it; similarly, by 'lower' is meant every genus or species that is under another, whether or not there is something under it, so that the intermediate genus is higher relative to what is below it and lower relative to what is above it. 'Universally' is a refutation of an objection: that every constituent of the higher is a constituent of the lower is a universal affirmative proposition, and its converse, 'some constituent of the lower is a constituent of the higher', is a particular affirmative necessarily true, so his statement 'no converse' has no meaning. The clarification of the refutation is that the commentator's saying 'universally' indicates that his saying 'no converse' is a negation of the universal affirmative, not the universal negative, and the negation of the universal affirmative does not contradict the particular affirmative. Every constituent is a proximate or remote differentia, so it cannot be objected that if by constituent is meant the proximate differentia, then no proximate constituent of the lower is a constituent of the higher, and if by constituent is meant the remote differentia, then every remote constituent of the lower is a constituent of the higher. And the divider is by converse, meaning that every divider of the lower is a divider of the higher, and there is no converse universally. As for the first, because the lower is a division of the higher, so every differentia that makes the lower a division makes the higher a division, because a division of a division is a division. As for the second, because, for example, 'sensitive' is a divider of the higher which is the growing body, but it is not a divider of the lower which is animal.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rule concerning the differentia's relationship to higher and lower genera. It clarifies two points: the 'no converse' rule for constituents (muqawwim) and the 'no converse' rule for dividers (muqassim). The author begins by explaining that when it is said 'no converse' regarding the differentia, it is meant as a universal negative: not every constituent of the lower is a constituent of the higher. The example given is 'rational' (natiq): it constitutes man (the lower) but not animal (the higher). He then defines 'higher' and 'lower' broadly to include intermediate genera, so that what is higher in one respect may be lower in another. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: if we assume the converse—that every constituent of the higher is a constituent of the lower—that would be a universal affirmative, and its opposite (some constituent of the lower is a constituent of the higher) is a particular affirmative that is true. So the statement 'no converse' does not negate that particular. The commentator clarifies that the negation is of the universal affirmative, not the universal negative. Next, the passage discusses the divider (muqassim): every divider of the lower is a divider of the higher, but not conversely. The reason is that the lower is a division of the higher, so any differentia that subdivides the lower also subdivides the higher. However, the example of 'sensitive' shows that a divider of the higher (growing body) need not be a divider of the lower (animal), since animal is not subdivided by 'sensitive' as animal is already defined by sensation. Thus, the differentia's function as a constituent and as a divider follows opposite converse patterns.

Key Terms
مقوم
مقسم
عالي
سافل

Checkpoint

A) Because animal is a species of sensitive.

D) Because animal is higher than sensitive.

Key Takeaway
The differentia behaves asymmetrically: a constituent of the lower may not be a constituent of the higher, but a divider of the lower always divides the higher, while the converse does not hold.

Special Characteristic vs Common Accident: Core Definitions
Defines special characteristic and common accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a special characteristic and a common accident based on their scope and relation to essence.

Core Idea
Special characteristic is exclusive to one reality; common accident spans multiple realities.

The fourth and fifth of the five universals are the special characteristic (khassa) and common accident ('arad 'amm). Both are external universals, but they differ in how broadly they apply. Understanding this distinction is critical for precise definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal as external
Check if the universal is a constituent of the essence (internal) or not (external). Both khassa and 'arad 'amm are external.
2
Determine its scope
Ask: Does this universal apply to everything under a single reality (species or genus) and nothing else? If yes, it is a special characteristic. If it applies to multiple different realities, it is a common accident.
Example: 'laughing' applies only to humans (single species), so it is a special characteristic. 'walker' applies to many animals, so it is a common accident for humans.
3
Check if it is a constituent of the higher
The passage states that common accident is 'not a constituent of the higher which is animal'. This means it is not part of the definition of a broader category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: الخاصة وهو الخارج المقول على ما تحت حقيقة واحدة فقط. الخامس: العرض العام وهو الخارج المقول عليها وليس مقوما للعالي الذي هو الحيوان قوله وهو الخارج: أي الكلي الخارج؛ فإن المقسم معتبر في جميع مفهومات الأقسام واعلم أن الخاصة تنقسم إلى خاصة شاملة لجميع أفراد ما هي خاصة له كالكاتب بالقوة للإنسان وإلى غير شاملة لجميع أفراده كالكاتب بالفعل للإنسان. قوله حقيقة واحدة نوعية أو جنسية، فالأول خاصة النوع، والثاني خاصة الجنس. فالماشي خاصة للحيوان وعرض عام للإنسان فافهم. كليا يعني أن هذا رفع الإيجاب الكلي، فيجوز أن يكون بعض مقسم العالي مقسم للسافل؛ فإن الناطق بانضمامه إلى الجوهر وجودا وعدما مقسم للجسم ومع ذلك مقسم للحيوان أيضاً. (عبد) قسم القسم: كما أن الناطق قسم للحيوان والحيوان قسم للنامي فالناطق قسم لقسم النامي وكل قسم لقسم النامي قسم للنامي فالناطق قسم للنامي. نوعية أو جنسية: دفع لما يتوهم في بادي الرأي من أن تعريف الخاصة غير مانع؛ لصدقه على العرض العام أيضا؛ فإنه يصدق عليه أنه خارج مقول على ما تحت حقيقة واحدة هي حقيقة الحيوان. حاصل الدفع أن الخاصة منقسمة إلى قسمين خاصة النوع: وهي الكلي الخارج المختص بالحقيقة النوعية الواحدة كالضاحك للإنسان. والثاني خاصة الجنس: وهي المقول على ما تحت حقيقة واحدة جنسية كالماشي للحيوان وإن كان عرضا عاما بالنسبة إلى الإنسان (إسماعيل) فافهم: إشارة إلى سؤال تقرير الأول: أن الخاصة والعرض العام إما يتباينان فكيف يجتمعان في مادة واحدة أولا فيبطل عدهما من الكلي؛ إذ الأقسام لابد أن تكون متباينة؟ وتحرير الجواب: أنا نختار الأول ونقول: إن اجتماع المتنافيين في مادة واحدة من جهتين ليس بمحال، فالماشي خاصة باعتبار أنه مختصة بالحقيقة الحيوانية وعرض عام للإنسان ولا مضايقة فيه؛ لأن الأحكام تختلف باختلاف الاعتبارات كالأبوة في زيد لعمرو والبنوة فيه لبكر. فتدبر.
The fourth: the special characteristic, which is the external universal predicated of what is under a single reality only. The fifth: the common accident, which is the external universal predicated of it and is not a constituent of the higher which is animal. His statement: 'which is external' i.e. the external universal; for the divider is considered in all the concepts of the divisions. Know that the special characteristic is divided into a special characteristic that encompasses all individuals of what it is special to, like 'writer in potentiality' for man, and one that does not encompass all its individuals, like 'writer in actuality' for man. His statement: 'a single reality, specific or generic', the first is the special characteristic of the species, and the second is the special characteristic of the genus. So 'walker' is a special characteristic for animal and a common accident for man, so understand. 'Universally' means that this is a negation of the universal affirmative, so it is possible that some divider of the higher is a divider of the lower; for 'rational', by being joined to substance in existence and non-existence, is a divider of body and yet is also a divider of animal. A division of a division: just as 'rational' is a division of animal and animal is a division of the growing, so 'rational' is a division of the division of the growing, and every division of the division of the growing is a division of the growing, so 'rational' is a division of the growing. 'Specific or generic' is a refutation of what might be imagined at first glance: that the definition of special characteristic is not exclusive because it applies to common accident as well; for it is true of common accident that it is external predicated of what is under a single reality, namely the reality of animal. The summary of the refutation is that special characteristic is divided into two: the special characteristic of the species, which is the external universal specific to one specific reality, like 'laughing' for man; and the second, the special characteristic of the genus, which is predicated of what is under a single generic reality, like 'walker' for animal, even if it is a common accident relative to man. (Isma'il) 'So understand' is an allusion to a question: the first formulation is that special characteristic and common accident are either mutually exclusive, so how can they come together in one matter? Or else their counting among the universals is invalid, since the divisions must be mutually exclusive. The answer: we choose the first and say: the combination of two opposites in one matter from two respects is not impossible. So 'walker' is a special characteristic in respect of being specific to the animal reality, and a common accident for man, and there is no difficulty in that, because judgments differ with differing considerations, like fatherhood in Zayd relative to 'Amr and sonship in him relative to Bakr. So ponder.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth and fifth universals: special characteristic (al-khassa) and common accident (al-'arad al-'amm). The author begins by stating that the special characteristic is an external universal (kulli kharj) that is predicated of things under a single reality only, meaning it applies exclusively to all members of a certain species or genus but not beyond. The common accident is also an external universal but is predicated across multiple realities; it is not a constituent of the higher (animal) but is still external. The commentary then explains the division of special characteristic into two types: comprehensive (shamil) like 'potential writer' for man, which applies to all men, and non-comprehensive like 'actual writer' which applies only to some. Additionally, special characteristic is divided by its subject: either specific (special to a species, like 'laughing' for man) or generic (special to a genus, like 'walker' for animal). The passage then addresses a key point: the same universal can be both a special characteristic in one respect and a common accident in another. For example, 'walker' is a special characteristic of animal (since only animals walk) but a common accident of man (since not all men walk, and it does not define man's essence). The commentary clarifies that this does not violate the mutual exclusivity of the universals because the classification depends on perspective. This is analogous to Zayd being a father to 'Amr and a son to Bakr—different relations do not contradict. The passage also includes a marginal note on the divider, but the main focus is the definitions and the resolution of the apparent conflict between special characteristic and common accident coinciding.

Key Terms
خاصة
عرض عام
كلي خارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Common accident is a constituent of the higher genus.

Key Takeaway
Special characteristic is exclusive to one reality; common accident spans multiple realities and is not definitionally constituent.

Types of Special Characteristic: Comprehensive and Non-Comprehensive
Divides special characteristic into two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a special characteristic as comprehensive or non-comprehensive and as species-specific or genus-specific.

Core Idea
Special characteristics can be comprehensive (applies to all individuals) or non-comprehensive (only to some).

After defining special characteristic, the source divides it into two major pairs: comprehensive vs non-comprehensive (based on coverage of individuals), and species-specific vs genus-specific (based on the type of reality). Understanding these subcategories refines your ability to analyze definitions.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish comprehensive from non-comprehensive
A special characteristic that applies to every individual of the species/genus is comprehensive (e.g., 'potential writer' applies to all humans). If it applies only to some, it is non-comprehensive (e.g., 'actual writer' applies only to those who write).
2
Distinguish species-specific from genus-specific
If the special characteristic is tied to a species (e.g., 'laughing' for human), it is a special characteristic of the species. If tied to a genus (e.g., 'walker' for animal), it is a special characteristic of the genus.
Note that 'walker' is a common accident for humans but a special characteristic of the genus animal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: الخاصة وهو الخارج المقول على ما تحت حقيقة واحدة فقط. الخامس: العرض العام وهو الخارج المقول عليها وليس مقوما للعالي الذي هو الحيوان قوله وهو الخارج: أي الكلي الخارج؛ فإن المقسم معتبر في جميع مفهومات الأقسام واعلم أن الخاصة تنقسم إلى خاصة شاملة لجميع أفراد ما هي خاصة له كالكاتب بالقوة للإنسان وإلى غير شاملة لجميع أفراده كالكاتب بالفعل للإنسان. قوله حقيقة واحدة نوعية أو جنسية، فالأول خاصة النوع، والثاني خاصة الجنس. فالماشي خاصة للحيوان وعرض عام للإنسان فافهم. كليا يعني أن هذا رفع الإيجاب الكلي، فيجوز أن يكون بعض مقسم العالي مقسم للسافل؛ فإن الناطق بانضمامه إلى الجوهر وجودا وعدما مقسم للجسم ومع ذلك مقسم للحيوان أيضاً. (عبد) قسم القسم: كما أن الناطق قسم للحيوان والحيوان قسم للنامي فالناطق قسم لقسم النامي وكل قسم لقسم النامي قسم للنامي فالناطق قسم للنامي. نوعية أو جنسية: دفع لما يتوهم في بادي الرأي من أن تعريف الخاصة غير مانع؛ لصدقه على العرض العام أيضا؛ فإنه يصدق عليه أنه خارج مقول على ما تحت حقيقة واحدة هي حقيقة الحيوان. حاصل الدفع أن الخاصة منقسمة إلى قسمين خاصة النوع: وهي الكلي الخارج المختص بالحقيقة النوعية الواحدة كالضاحك للإنسان. والثاني خاصة الجنس: وهي المقول على ما تحت حقيقة واحدة جنسية كالماشي للحيوان وإن كان عرضا عاما بالنسبة إلى الإنسان (إسماعيل) فافهم: إشارة إلى سؤال تقرير الأول: أن الخاصة والعرض العام إما يتباينان فكيف يجتمعان في مادة واحدة أولا فيبطل عدهما من الكلي؛ إذ الأقسام لابد أن تكون متباينة؟ وتحرير الجواب: أنا نختار الأول ونقول: إن اجتماع المتنافيين في مادة واحدة من جهتين ليس بمحال، فالماشي خاصة باعتبار أنه مختصة بالحقيقة الحيوانية وعرض عام للإنسان ولا مضايقة فيه؛ لأن الأحكام تختلف باختلاف الاعتبارات كالأبوة في زيد لعمرو والبنوة فيه لبكر. فتدبر.
The fourth: the special characteristic, which is the external universal predicated of what is under a single reality only. The fifth: the common accident, which is the external universal predicated of it and is not a constituent of the higher which is animal. His statement: 'which is external' i.e. the external universal; for the divider is considered in all the concepts of the divisions. Know that the special characteristic is divided into a special characteristic that encompasses all individuals of what it is special to, like 'writer in potentiality' for man, and one that does not encompass all its individuals, like 'writer in actuality' for man. His statement: 'a single reality, specific or generic', the first is the special characteristic of the species, and the second is the special characteristic of the genus. So 'walker' is a special characteristic for animal and a common accident for man, so understand. 'Universally' means that this is a negation of the universal affirmative, so it is possible that some divider of the higher is a divider of the lower; for 'rational', by being joined to substance in existence and non-existence, is a divider of body and yet is also a divider of animal. A division of a division: just as 'rational' is a division of animal and animal is a division of the growing, so 'rational' is a division of the division of the growing, and every division of the division of the growing is a division of the growing, so 'rational' is a division of the growing. 'Specific or generic' is a refutation of what might be imagined at first glance: that the definition of special characteristic is not exclusive because it applies to common accident as well; for it is true of common accident that it is external predicated of what is under a single reality, namely the reality of animal. The summary of the refutation is that special characteristic is divided into two: the special characteristic of the species, which is the external universal specific to one specific reality, like 'laughing' for man; and the second, the special characteristic of the genus, which is predicated of what is under a single generic reality, like 'walker' for animal, even if it is a common accident relative to man. (Isma'il) 'So understand' is an allusion to a question: the first formulation is that special characteristic and common accident are either mutually exclusive, so how can they come together in one matter? Or else their counting among the universals is invalid, since the divisions must be mutually exclusive. The answer: we choose the first and say: the combination of two opposites in one matter from two respects is not impossible. So 'walker' is a special characteristic in respect of being specific to the animal reality, and a common accident for man, and there is no difficulty in that, because judgments differ with differing considerations, like fatherhood in Zayd relative to 'Amr and sonship in him relative to Bakr. So ponder.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth and fifth universals: special characteristic (al-khassa) and common accident (al-'arad al-'amm). The author begins by stating that the special characteristic is an external universal (kulli kharj) that is predicated of things under a single reality only, meaning it applies exclusively to all members of a certain species or genus but not beyond. The common accident is also an external universal but is predicated across multiple realities; it is not a constituent of the higher (animal) but is still external. The commentary then explains the division of special characteristic into two types: comprehensive (shamil) like 'potential writer' for man, which applies to all men, and non-comprehensive like 'actual writer' which applies only to some. Additionally, special characteristic is divided by its subject: either specific (special to a species, like 'laughing' for man) or generic (special to a genus, like 'walker' for animal). The passage then addresses a key point: the same universal can be both a special characteristic in one respect and a common accident in another. For example, 'walker' is a special characteristic of animal (since only animals walk) but a common accident of man (since not all men walk, and it does not define man's essence). The commentary clarifies that this does not violate the mutual exclusivity of the universals because the classification depends on perspective. This is analogous to Zayd being a father to 'Amr and a son to Bakr—different relations do not contradict. The passage also includes a marginal note on the divider, but the main focus is the definitions and the resolution of the apparent conflict between special characteristic and common accident coinciding.

Key Terms
خاصة شاملة
خاصة غير شاملة
خاصة النوع
خاصة الجنس
ماشي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between a comprehensive and non-comprehensive special characteristic?
A) Comprehensive applies to all species, non-comprehensive applies to one genus.
B) Comprehensive applies to all individuals of the species/genus, non-comprehensive applies to only some.
C) Comprehensive is always genus-specific, non-comprehensive is species-specific.
D) There is no difference; both terms are synonymous.

Key Takeaway
Special characteristics are classified by coverage (all or some individuals) and by the type of reality (species or genus).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the attribute 'being able to marry' as comprehensive or non-comprehensive for the species human, and determine whether it is a special characteristic of the species or genus.
?
Use the example from the text: 'walker' is a special characteristic of animal but a common accident of human. Explain why it is non-comprehensive for human? (Hint: not all humans walk at all times).

Resolving the Conflict: One Universal as Both Khassa and 'Arad 'Amm
Resolves the objection that the same universal cannot be both khassa and 'arad 'amm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve the apparent contradiction when the same universal is both a special characteristic and a common accident by applying the principle of differing considerations.

Core Idea
A universal can be both special and common from different perspectives without contradiction.

A student might object: how can the same universal be both a special characteristic and a common accident? Are the five universals not mutually exclusive? The source anticipates this and provides a clear answer using the example of 'walker' and the principle that judgments differ with differing considerations (ikhtilaf al-i'tibar). This lesson analyzes that dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent conflict
The universal 'walker' is stated to be a special characteristic of animal (because only animals walk) and a common accident of man (because other animals walk too and it is not part of man's essence). This seems to violate the exclusivity of the divisions.
2
Recognize the two perspectives
When considering 'walker' relative to the genus animal, it is a special characteristic. When considering it relative to the species human, it is a common accident. The classification depends on the relationship.
The text says: 'فالماشي خاصة للحيوان وعرض عام للإنسان'
3
Apply the principle of differing considerations (i'tibar)
The commentary uses the analogy of Zayd being a father to 'Amr and a son to Bakr. Just as these roles are not contradictory because they are from different relations, so too can a universal have different classifications from different perspectives.
The text says: 'لأن الأحكام تختلف باختلاف الاعتبارات'

Source Text

Defining the Peculiar Property and General Accident
Defines the fourth and fifth universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the peculiar property (khāṣṣah) from the general accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm) based on their definitions and predicate scope.

Core Idea
The peculiar property belongs to one essence; the general accident spreads across many without being constitutive.

In this passage, the author defines the fourth and fifth universals from the Isagoge-based classification. Understanding these two helps you see how predicates can be exclusive to one category or shared across many. Pay attention to the phrase 'external' (al-khārij) and the condition 'not constitutive of the higher genus'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definienda
Find the terms 'الخاصة' and 'العرض العام' in the Arabic. Note that they are the fourth and fifth universals.
2
Extract the common genus
Both are defined as 'al-khārij' (the external). This means they are not part of the essence of what they are predicated on; they are accidental.
3
Identify the differentia for each
For الخاصة: 'مقول على ما تحت حقيقة واحدة فقط' – predicated on only one single essence. For العرض العام: 'مقول عليها وليس مقوما للعالي' – predicated on multiple essences and not constitutive of the higher genus.
The differentia for الخاصة is exclusivity; for العرض العام it is non-constitutive multiplicity.
4
Apply to fiqh examples
Think of 'wujūb' (obligation) as a general accident because it applies to many actions. 'Takbīr al-ihrām' (opening takbir) is a peculiar property of prayer because it is specific to it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف الخاصة والعرض العام والمقسم بالعكس. الرابع: الخاصة وهو الخارج المقول على ما تحت حقيقة واحدة فقط. الخامس: العرض العام وهو الخارج المقول عليها وليس مقوما للعالي الذي هو الحيوان. قوله والمقسم بالعكس أي كل مقسم للسافل مقسم للعالي ولا عكس أي كليا. أما الأول؛ فلأن السافل قسم من العالي، فكل فصل حصل للسافل قسما فقد حصل للعالي قسما؛ لأن قسم القسم قسم. وأما الثاني؛ فلأن الحساس مثلا مقسم للعالي الذي هو الجسم النامي وليس مقسما للسافل الذي هو الحيوان. قوله: وهو الخارج: أي الكلي الخارج؛ فإن المقسم معتبر في جميع مفهومات الأقسام.
Definition of the peculiar property and the general accident and the division by opposite. Fourth: The peculiar property, which is the external (predicate) predicated on what falls under a single essence only. Fifth: The general accident, which is the external predicated upon them and not constitutive of the higher (genus) which is 'animal'. His statement: 'and the division by opposite' means: every divisor of the lower is a divisor of the higher, and not conversely, i.e., universally. As for the first: because the lower is a part of the higher, so every differentia that makes the lower a division makes the higher a division, for the part of a part is a part. As for the second: because the sensitive, for example, is a divisor of the higher which is the growing body, but it is not a divisor of the lower which is the animal. His statement: 'and it is external' means: the external universal; for the divisor is considered in all the conceptualizations of the divisions.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth and fifth universals in the Isagoge-based classification. The author states that the peculiar property (al-khassa) is a universal that is predicated on only one specific essence (e.g., risibility for humans), while the general accident (al-'arad al-'amm) is predicated on multiple essences but is not constitutive of the higher genus (e.g., animal). The author then explains the rule of 'division by opposite' (al-muqassam bi al-'aks): any divisor (differentia) that divides the lower (a species) also divides the higher (the genus), but not vice versa. The reason given is that the lower is a part of the higher; if a differentia causes the lower to be divided, it also causes the higher to be divided because 'the part of a part is a part'. The second part of the rule is illustrated by the example of 'sensitive' (al-hassas): it divides the growing body (a genus) but does not divide animal (a species within that genus). Finally, the author clarifies that 'external' in the definition refers to the external universal, meaning the divisor is considered in all conceptualizations of the divisions.

Key Terms
خاصة
عرض عام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is 'walking' (المشي) considered a general accident rather than a peculiar property?
A) Because it is predicated on only one essence.
B) Because it is predicated on multiple essences and not constitutive of the higher genus.
C) Because it is an external universal.
D) Because it divides the lower and higher.

Key Takeaway
The peculiar property is exclusive to one essence; the general accident is shared across many.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the legal term 'wujūb' (obligation). Is it a khassa or arad 'amm? Explain: it applies to many actions, so it is a general accident.
?
Now consider the term 'takbīr al-ihrām' (the opening takbir in prayer). Is it a khassa or arad 'amm? It is specific to prayer, so it is a peculiar property.

The Rule of Division by Opposite
Explains the rule of division by opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that any divisor of a lower division also divides the higher division, but not conversely.

Core Idea
Divide the species and you divide the genus; but dividing the genus may not cut the species.

This passage introduces a logical rule about how differentiae (fusul) divide categories. The author explains that if a differentia divides a lower category (a species), it also divides the higher category (the genus). However, a differentia that divides the genus does not necessarily divide the species. Understanding this rule is crucial for correct classification in any Islamic science that uses hierarchical categories.

Guided Path
1
Understand the terms
The rule uses 'muqassam' (divisor/differentia), 'safil' (lower = species), and 'ali' (higher = genus). A divisor makes a division within a category.
2
Grasp the first direction
If a divisor divides the lower, it must divide the higher. Reason: lower is a part of higher; if a differentia cuts the part, it cuts the whole.
3
Grasp the second direction (the opposite)
But a divisor of the higher may not divide the lower. Example: 'sensitive' (al-hassas) divides the growing body (a genus) but does not divide animal (a species within that genus).
4
Test with a fiqh example
Consider 'purification' (ṭahārah) divided into 'wudū' and 'tayammum'. The differentia 'involves water' divides the genus (purification) but not the species tayammum. This illustrates the 'not conversely' part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف الخاصة والعرض العام والمقسم بالعكس. الرابع: الخاصة وهو الخارج المقول على ما تحت حقيقة واحدة فقط. الخامس: العرض العام وهو الخارج المقول عليها وليس مقوما للعالي الذي هو الحيوان. قوله والمقسم بالعكس أي كل مقسم للسافل مقسم للعالي ولا عكس أي كليا. أما الأول؛ فلأن السافل قسم من العالي، فكل فصل حصل للسافل قسما فقد حصل للعالي قسما؛ لأن قسم القسم قسم. وأما الثاني؛ فلأن الحساس مثلا مقسم للعالي الذي هو الجسم النامي وليس مقسما للسافل الذي هو الحيوان. قوله: وهو الخارج: أي الكلي الخارج؛ فإن المقسم معتبر في جميع مفهومات الأقسام.
Definition of the peculiar property and the general accident and the division by opposite. Fourth: The peculiar property, which is the external (predicate) predicated on what falls under a single essence only. Fifth: The general accident, which is the external predicated upon them and not constitutive of the higher (genus) which is 'animal'. His statement: 'and the division by opposite' means: every divisor of the lower is a divisor of the higher, and not conversely, i.e., universally. As for the first: because the lower is a part of the higher, so every differentia that makes the lower a division makes the higher a division, for the part of a part is a part. As for the second: because the sensitive, for example, is a divisor of the higher which is the growing body, but it is not a divisor of the lower which is the animal. His statement: 'and it is external' means: the external universal; for the divisor is considered in all the conceptualizations of the divisions.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth and fifth universals in the Isagoge-based classification. The author states that the peculiar property (al-khassa) is a universal that is predicated on only one specific essence (e.g., risibility for humans), while the general accident (al-'arad al-'amm) is predicated on multiple essences but is not constitutive of the higher genus (e.g., animal). The author then explains the rule of 'division by opposite' (al-muqassam bi al-'aks): any divisor (differentia) that divides the lower (a species) also divides the higher (the genus), but not vice versa. The reason given is that the lower is a part of the higher; if a differentia causes the lower to be divided, it also causes the higher to be divided because 'the part of a part is a part'. The second part of the rule is illustrated by the example of 'sensitive' (al-hassas): it divides the growing body (a genus) but does not divide animal (a species within that genus). Finally, the author clarifies that 'external' in the definition refers to the external universal, meaning the divisor is considered in all conceptualizations of the divisions.

Key Terms
مقسم بالعكس
السافل
العالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the differentia 'sensitive' (al-ḥassās) not divide the lower (animal) according to the passage?
A) Because sensitive is not a real property of animals.
B) Because animal is a part of the growing body, so the differentia must apply to both.
C) Because the rule says the converse is not generally true; a divisor of the higher may not divide the lower.
D) Because sensitive is a genus and not a differentia.

Key Takeaway
A divisor of a species always divides its genus, but a divisor of a genus does not always divide its species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Comprehensive vs Non-Comprehensive Properties
Explains the division of property into comprehensive and non-comprehensive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between comprehensive and non-comprehensive properties using Islamic logical examples.

Core Idea
Not every property covers all individuals; some apply only to a subset.

The peculiar property (al-khassa) divides into two types based on coverage. Understanding this helps avoid confusing essential attributes with accidental ones. The passage gives the examples of 'potential writer' (comprehensive) and 'actual writer' (non-comprehensive) for humans.

Guided Path
1
Identify the property and its subject
Look at the property being examined and determine which thing it is a property for (e.g., 'potential writer' for humans).
2
Check coverage over all individuals
Ask: Does this property apply to every single individual of that species? If yes, it is comprehensive; if not, it is non-comprehensive.
Example: 'Potential writer' applies to all humans because all have the capacity to write, even if not actualized.
3
Classify accordingly
Label the property as shāmila (comprehensive) or ghayr shāmila (non-comprehensive).
Non-comprehensive properties are accidental and do not define the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الخاصة تنقسم إلى خاصة شاملة لجميع أفراد ما هي خاصة له كالكاتب بالقوة للإنسان وإلى غير شاملة لجميع أفراده كالكاتب بالفعل للإنسان. قوله: حقيقة واحدة نوعية أو جنسية، فالأول خاصة النوع، والثاني خاصة الجنس. فالماشي خاصة للحيوان وعرض عام للإنسان فافهم.
And know that the peculiar property is divided into a property comprehensive of all individuals of that for which it is a property, like potential writer for man, and into one not comprehensive of all its individuals, like actual writer for man. His statement: 'a single essence, specific or generic', so the first is a specific property, and the second is a generic property. So the walker is a property for animal and a general accident for man. So understand.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage expands on the definition of the peculiar property (al-khassa). The author explains that the peculiar property can be divided in two ways. First, it can be comprehensive (shāmila), meaning it applies to every individual of the species (e.g., 'potential writer' applies to all humans), or non-comprehensive (ghayr shāmila), meaning it applies only to some individuals (e.g., 'actual writer' applies only to those humans who actually write). Second, the property can be 'specific property' (khassat al-naw‘), which is restricted to one species (e.g., risibility for humans), or 'generic property' (khassat al-jins), which applies to one genus but not beyond (e.g., 'walker' for animal). The example 'walker' is a property for animal (since only animals walk) but a general accident for humans (since humans walk but also have other properties). The final 'fafham' urges the student to grasp this distinction carefully.

Key Terms
شاملة
غير شاملة

Key Takeaway
A comprehensive property applies to all individuals of the species, while a non-comprehensive property applies only to some.

Specific vs Generic Properties
Explains the division of property into specific and generic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between a specific property (khāssat al-naw‘) and a generic property (khāssat al-jins) using Islamic logical examples.

Core Idea
Properties can be unique to a single species or shared by an entire genus.

The peculiar property also divides by scope: some are specific to one species (e.g., risibility for humans), while others are generic, applying to the whole genus (e.g., walker for animal). This distinction refines our understanding of definitions and prevents misclassification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the property and its subject
Look at the property and determine which species or genus it characterizes (e.g., 'walker' for animal as a genus).
2
Determine the scope
Ask: Does this property apply to only one species? If yes, it is a specific property (khāssat al-naw‘). Does it apply to a whole genus but not beyond? If yes, it is a generic property (khāssat al-jins).
Example: 'Risibility' is specific to humans; 'walker' is generic for animal (all animals walk, but not all beings).
3
Classify accordingly
Label the property as specific or generic based on its scope.
Note: A generic property for a genus may be a general accident for a species within that genus, as 'walker' is a general accident for humans.

Resolving the Overlap of Property and General Accident
Dialectical objection and answer about khāssa and ʿaraḍ ʿāmm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve the apparent contradiction of a universal being both a peculiar property and a general accident by applying the principle of multiple considerations (iʿtibārāt).

Core Idea
Predication is relative: one universal can be a property for one genus and a general accident for another.

A student of logic might notice that a universal like 'walker' is at once a peculiar property (khāssa) for the genus animal and a general accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm) for the species human. This seems to violate the rule that the five universals are mutually exclusive. This lesson examines the objection and its resolution, which hinges on recognizing that relations (iʿtibārāt) can differ without contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Identify the core problem: If khāssa and ʿaraḍ ʿāmm are mutually exclusive, how can one universal (e.g., 'walker') be both? If they are not exclusive, the division of the five universals collapses.
The objection is dialectical – it questions the coherence of the classification.
2
Identify the counterexample
Notice the specific case: 'walker' (al-māshī) is presented as a khāssa for animal (because only animals walk) and a ʿaraḍ ʿāmm for human (because many other animals also walk).
The same term is used as an example in the passage.
3
Grasp the resolution via multiple considerations
The author resolves the contradiction by pointing out that the two classifications arise from different points of view (iʿtibārāt). For animal, 'walker' is a property; for human, it is a general accident. No contradiction arises because the aspects differ.
This principle is a key tool in logic and legal theory: a thing can have two distinct relations without conflict.
4
Apply the analogy
Consider the analogue given: Zayd can be both a father (to Amr) and a son (to Bakr) simultaneously. This is possible because the two relations are directed at different individuals. Similarly, 'walker' relates to animal and human differently.
The analogy reinforces the logical point and makes it intuitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فافهم: إشارة إلى سؤال تقرير الأول: أن الخاصة والعرض العام إما يتباينان فكيف يجتمعان في مادة واحدة أولا فيبطل عدهما من الكلي؛ إذ الأقسام لابد أن تكون متباينة؟ وتحرير الجواب: أنا نختار الأول ونقول: إن اجتماع المتنافيين في مادة واحدة من جهتين ليس بمحال، فالماشي خاصة باعتبار أنه مختصة بالحقيقة الحيوانية وعرض عام للإنسان ولا مضايقة فيه؛ لأن الأحكام تختلف باختلاف الاعتبارات كالأبوة في زيد لعمرو والبنوة فيه لبكر. فتدبر.
So understand: an indication of a question whose formulation is: that the peculiar property and the general accident are either mutually exclusive — so how can they combine in a single subject? — or they are not, which would invalidate their being counted among the universals, since divisions must be mutually exclusive. And the composition of the answer: we choose the first (position) and say: the combination of two opposites in a single subject from two aspects is not impossible. Thus 'walker' is a property in that it is specific to animality, and a general accident for man; there is no conflict, because judgments differ according to different considerations, like fatherhood in Zayd relative to Amr and sonship in him relative to Bakr. So reflect.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical objection and its answer. The objection is: the peculiar property and the general accident are supposed to be mutually exclusive (since one is restricted to one essence, the other is broader). But we find that one and the same universal (e.g., 'walker') is both a property (for the genus animal) and a general accident (for the species human). How can that be? If they are not exclusive, then the division of universals into five fails. The answer given is that the same universal can be a property under one consideration (its relation to the genus animal, where it is exclusively predicated) and a general accident under another consideration (its relation to humans, where it is also predicated but not exclusively). This is not contradictory; it is analogous to how the same person can be a father relative to one person and a son relative to another. The author concludes with 'fafham' and 'fataddabar' urging deep reflection.

Key Terms
خاصة
عرض عام

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) By saying that 'property' and 'general accident' are not mutually exclusive categories.
C) By pointing out that 'walker' is a property relative to animal and a general accident relative to man.
D) By adding a new universal category.

Key Takeaway
The same universal can belong to different logical categories when considered under different relations (iʿtibārāt), preserving the exclusivity of the five universals.

Three Types of Necessary Concomitant and Their Reduction to Two Categories
Author classifies necessary concomitant into three types and explains they reduce to two categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a necessary concomitant as essential (lāzim al-māhiyya) or existential (lāzim al-wujūd), and distinguish between external and mental existence concomitants.

Core Idea
Three types reduce to two: essential and existential.

This passage from the commentary on Tahdhīb al-Manṭiq clarifies a subtle point about the division of necessary concomitants (lāzim). The author lists three types—essential, external existence, and mental existence—but then explains that the latter two are actually a single category 'existential' (lāzim al-wujūd). Understanding this reduction is important for correctly analyzing definitions and properties of quiddities in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types mentioned
Find the three types of necessary concomitant listed in the passage: lāzim al-māhiyya (e.g., evenness of four), lāzim al-wujūd al-khārijī (e.g., burning of fire), and lāzim al-wujūd al-dhihnī (e.g., universality of man).
The third type is also called ma‘qūl thānī.
2
Understand the dual division
Notice that the passage explicitly states 'this section is actually two sections'. The two categories are lāzim al-māhiyya (essential) and lāzim al-wujūd (existential).
3
Analyze the commentator’s explanation
Read the commentator’s defense: the author did not abandon the well-known triple division; he merely combined the two existence-related types under one term for brevity. Thus, lāzim al-wujūd encompasses both external and mental existence.
The commentator is Ismā‘īl, likely a gloss on the main text.
4
Apply the classification
For each example given, determine whether it is essential or existential. For instance, 'evenness of four' is essential; 'burning of fire' is existential (external); 'universality of man' is existential (mental).
Consider whether the property follows from the essence itself or from its existence in reality or mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا القسم بالحقيقة قسمان فأقسام اللازم بهذا التقسيم ثلاثة: لازم الماهية كزوجية الأربعة، ولازم الوجود الخارجي كإحراق النار، ولازم الوجود الذهني ككون حقيقة الإنسان كلية، فهذا القسم يسمى معقولا ثانيا أيضاً. فهذا القسم إلخ: دفع لما يكاد أن يتوهم أن المصنف لم عدل عن القسمة الثلاثية المشهورة التي هي لازم الوجود الخارجي ولازم الوجود الذهني ولازم الماهية إلى الثنائية التي هي لازم الماهية ولازم الوجود، وحاصل الدفع ما عدل عنه بل غير عن القسمين الأخيرين بعبارة واحدة للاختصار فلازم الوجود عام شامل للازم الوجود الذهني ولازم الوجود الخارجي. إسماعيل
Thus this section is actually two sections, so the divisions of the necessary concomitant according to this division are three: necessary concomitant of the essence like the evenness of four, necessary concomitant of external existence like the burning of fire, and necessary concomitant of mental existence like the universality of the reality of man; this section is also called a secondary intelligible. 'Thus this section, etc.': A refutation of the suspicion that the author changed from the well-known triple division (external existence, mental existence, and essence) to the dual division (essence and existence). The gist of the refutation is that he did not change; rather, he merely combined the two latter categories under one expression for brevity, so 'necessary concomitant of existence' is general, including both mental and external existence. (Isma'il)
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the division of the necessary concomitant (اللازم). It states that the section is actually two sections, and then lists three types: (1) necessary concomitant of the essence (لازم الماهية), e.g., that four is even; (2) necessary concomitant of external existence (لازم الوجود الخارجي), e.g., fire burns; (3) necessary concomitant of mental existence (لازم الوجود الذهني), e.g., man is a universal. The third type is also called a 'secondary intelligible' (معقول ثان). The commentator then anticipates a potential misunderstanding: that the author deviated from the usual threefold division (essence, external existence, mental existence) to a twofold division (essence and existence). The response is that the author did not change; he simply grouped the two types of existence under one heading 'lazim al-wujud' for conciseness. Thus the three types reduce to two categories: lazim al-mahiyya (essential) and lazim al-wujud (existential), with the latter encompassing both external and mental existence.

Key Terms
لازم الماهية (lāzim al-māhiyya): The necessary concomitant that flows from the essence itself, such as evenness for four. [Note: Stable across logical texts.]
لازم الوجود (lāzim al-wujūd): The necessary concomitant that follows from existence, either external or mental. Here it is used as a general category encompassing both. [Note: In some texts, 'lazim al-wujud' may refer only to external existence; the commentary explicitly broadens it.]
معقول ثان (ma‘qūl thānī): An accident that attaches to a quiddity when it exists in the mind, like universality for man. [Note: In later ontology, this term has broader usage, but here it is specifically the mental concomitant.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One of the three types is invalid.
B) The author made a mistake and corrected it.
C) The two existence types are combined under one term for brevity.
D) The three types are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
The three types of necessary concomitant reduce to two: essential (lāzim al-māhiyya) and existential (lāzim al-wujūd), with existential encompassing both external and mental existence.
