The Two Senses of Evident Necessary Concomitant
Defining two senses of evident and non-evident necessary concomitants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the specific and general senses of 'evident' (bayyin) and their corresponding 'non-evident' (ghayr bayyin) in the context of necessary concomitants.

Core Idea
Evident means either immediate conception or immediate judgment; the specific sense is narrower than the general.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the division of necessary concomitants into evident and non-evident, but reveals that 'evident' has two distinct meanings. Understanding these layers is crucial for precise definition in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Find where the author defines 'evident in the specific sense' (بين بالمعنى الأخص) and 'evident in the general sense' (بين بالمعنى الأعم). Note the criteria: for the specific, the concept follows from the necessitating thing alone; for the general, the judgment of necessity follows from conceptualizing both terms and their relation.
The definitions appear early in the passage: 'والثاني أن اللازم إما بين أو غير بين...'
2
Extract examples
For the specific sense, the example is sight from blindness (تصور البصر من تصور العمى). For the general sense, the example is evenness of four (زوجية الأربعة). Note that the intellect judges that evenness is necessary for four after conceiving both.
3
Derive non-evident for each sense
Non-evident in the specific sense is a concomitant whose concept does not follow from the necessitating thing alone, like the potential for writing in man. Non-evident in the general sense is a concomitant where judgment of necessity does not follow from conceptualizing both terms and their relation, like the temporality of the world.
The commentator explicitly states each non-evident definition.
4
Analyze the relationship between the two senses
The commentator notes that the specific sense is not necessarily a subset of the general sense. For a concomitant to be evident in the specific sense, its concept must follow immediately; but it may still require a middle term for the judgment of necessity, so it may not be evident in the general sense. Thus they have a general-specific relationship in some respects.
This is a key nuance: the two senses are not simply one broader and one narrower.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن اللازم إما بين أو غير بين، والبين له معنيان: أحدهما: الذي يلزم تصوره من تصور الملزوم كما يلزم تصور البصر من تصور العمى، فهذا ما يقال له بين بالمعنى الأخص وحينئذ فغير البين هو اللازم الذي لا يلزم تصوره من تصور الملزوم كالكتابة بالقوة للإنسان، والثاني من معنى البين هو اللازم الذي يلزم من تصوره مع تصور الملزوم، والنسبة بينهما الجزمُ باللزوم كزوجية الأربعة، فإن العقل بعد تصور الأربعة والزوجية ونسبة الزوجية إليها يحكم جزما بأن الزوجية لازمة لها، وذلك يقال له : البين بالمعنى الأعم، وحينئذ فغير البين هو اللازم الذي لا يلزم من تصوره مع تصور الملزومِ والنسبة بينهما الجزم باللزوم كالحدوث للعالم، فهذا التقسيم الثاني بالحقيقة تقسيمان إلا أن القسمين الحاصلين على كل تقدير إنما يسميان بالبين وغير البين. قوله يدوم كحركة الفلك؛ فإنها دائمة للفلك وإن لم يمتنع انفكاكها عنه بالنظر إلى ذاته قوله بسرعة كحمرة الخجل وصفرة الوجل. قوله: أو بطوء: بالمعلن الأعم: يصدق البين بالمعنى الثاني على كل ما يصدق عليه بالمعنى الأول؛ فإن كل ما يلزم تصوره من تصور الملزوم يكون بحيث يلزم من تصوره وتصور ملزومه وتصور النسبة بينهما الجزم باللزوم بالضرورة، وفيه منع ظاهر؛ الجواز لازم يلزم تصوره من تصور ملزومه ويكون الجزم باللزوم موقوفا على وسط فبينهما عموم من وجه للمطلق، نعم! أن المعنى الثاني إنما يكون أعم والأول أخص إذا اعتبر في الأول ما اعتبر في الثاني وكون تصور هما مع النسبة كافيا في الجزم باللزوم مع أنهم لم يعتبروه في الأول كما ذكره جلال العلماء. (عبد) باللزوم: أي لا يلزم الجزم باللزوم بل يكون الجزم موقوفا على آخر. واعلم أن غير البين بالمعنى الأعم أخص من غير البين بالمعنى الأخص؛ لأن نقيض الأخص أعم من نقيض الأعم كما مر . (عبد) كالحدوث للعالم: فإنا إذا تصورنا الحدوث والعالم والنسبة بينهما لا يكفي للجزم باللزوم بل يحتاج إلى الدليل. (برهان الدين) فهذا التقسيم ههنا محل إيراد وهو أن المفهوم من كلام المصنف أن للبين معنى واحد هو المفهوم المردد وكذا الغير البين مع أن للبين معنيين على حدتين في الواقع، كما وقع في تفسير الجماهير، وأيضاً شرح الشارح خلاف ما في المتن وموافقا للمشهور فادعى إلى دفعه بأن المطلوب كما قلت لكن هذا التقسيم الثاني في الحقيقة تقسيمان: الأول تقسيم اللازم إلى البين بالمعنى الأخص وإلى غير البين بالمعنى الأخص والثاني في تقسيمه إلى البين بالمعنى الأعم وإلى غير البين بالمعنى الأعم لكن المصنف؛ لاشتراك إطلاق لفظ البين على الأخص والأعم وكذا لاشتراك غير البين جعله في صورة تقسيم واحد برهان دائمة للفلك فيه أن الحركة يمتنع انفكاكها عن الفلك ما دام وجود العلة فتكون ضرورية لازمة لا عرضا مفارقا، وقد يجاب عنه بأنه يستلزم أن تكون الدائمة مساوية الضرورية؛ فإن كل دائم لابد له من سبب يكون هو يمنع الانفكاك مادام وجوده، وهذا دقة فلسفية والكلام ههنا مبني على ما هو المشهور من عموم الدائمة من الضرورية. فتدبر. (عبد الحليم)
The second [division] is that the necessary concomitant is either evident or non-evident. Evident has two meanings: one is that whose conception follows from the conception of the necessitating thing, just as the conception of vision follows from the conception of blindness; this is called evident in the specific sense. Then non-evident is the necessary concomitant whose conception does not follow from the conception of the necessitating thing, like the potential for writing in man. The second meaning of evident is the necessary concomitant such that from its conception together with the conception of the necessitating thing and the relation between them, a judgment of necessity follows, like the evenness of four. For the intellect, after conceiving of four, evenness, and the relation of evenness to it, judges with certainty that evenness is necessary for it. This is called evident in the general sense. Then non-evident is the necessary concomitant such that from its conception together with the conception of the necessitating thing and the relation between them, a judgment of necessity does not follow, like the temporality of the world. Thus this second division is actually two divisions, except that the resulting divisions in each case are called evident and non-evident. His saying: 'endures' like the motion of the celestial sphere, for it is perpetual for the sphere, even if its separation from it is not impossible with respect to its essence. His saying: 'quickly' like the redness of embarrassment and the pallor of fear. His saying: 'or slowly'... In the general sense: the evident in the second sense applies to everything that applies to it in the first sense, for everything whose conception follows from the conception of the necessitating thing is such that from its conception and the conception of its necessitating thing and the conception of the relation between them, the judgment of necessity necessarily follows. But there is an obvious objection: it is possible that a necessary concomitant whose conception follows from the conception of its necessitating thing but the judgment of necessity depends on a middle term, so there is a general-specific relationship between them in some respects. Yes! The second meaning is more general and the first more specific if we consider in the first what is considered in the second, i.e., that conceptualizing both with the relation is sufficient for the judgment of necessity, whereas they did not consider that in the first, as mentioned by Jalal al-'Ulama'. (Abd) 'By necessity': i.e., the judgment of necessity does not follow, but rather the judgment depends on something else. Know that the non-evident in the general sense is more specific than the non-evident in the specific sense, because the negation of the specific is more general than the negation of the general, as has passed. (Abd) 'Like the temporality of the world': for if we conceive of temporality, the world, and the relation between them, it is not sufficient for the judgment of necessity; rather it needs a proof. (Burhan al-Din) So this division here is a point of objection: that the understanding from the author's words is that 'evident' has one meaning, namely the ambiguous concept, and likewise 'non-evident', while in fact 'evident' has two distinct meanings, as occurs in the interpretation of the majority. Also the commentator's explanation contradicts the text and agrees with the common view. So he claimed to refute it by saying that what is intended is as I said, but this second division is actually two divisions: one is the division of the necessary concomitant into the evident in the specific sense and the non-evident in the specific sense, and the second is its division into the evident in the general sense and the non-evident in the general sense. However, the author—due to the common usage of the term 'evident' for both the specific and general, and similarly for 'non-evident'—presented it in the form of a single division. A proof that the motion of the celestial sphere is perpetual: that motion cannot be separated from the sphere as long as the cause exists, so it is necessary and necessary, not a separating accident. And it may be answered that this would entail that the perpetual is equivalent to the necessary; for everything perpetual must have a cause that prevents separation as long as it exists. This is a philosophical subtlety, and the discussion here is based on the common notion that what is perpetual is [generally considered] necessary. So reflect. (Abd al-Halim)
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second division of the necessary concomitant (اللازم) into evident (بين) and non-evident (غير بين). The commentator first provides two meanings of 'evident': the specific sense (الأخص) where merely conceptualizing the necessitating thing yields the concept of the concomitant (e.g., blindness yields the concept of sight), and the general sense (الأعم) where conceptualizing both terms and their relation yields a judgment of necessity (e.g., evenness of four). For each, the non-evident is the opposite. The commentator then explains that this single division actually comprises two distinct divisions, one for each sense, but they share the same labels. Moving to examples, the commentator discusses the phrase 'it endures' (يدوم) with the motion of the celestial sphere as an enduring concomitant, and gives examples of quickly changing (blushing) and slowly changing (pallor) attributes. Then there is a critique: the author's wording suggests only one meaning of 'evident', but there are two. The response is that the author combined the divisions due to the common terminology. A further point is made about the relation between the two senses: the specific sense is not necessarily a subset of the general sense because judgment of necessity might require a middle term. The commentary ends with a philosophical discussion about whether the perpetual motion of the sphere is truly necessary or merely enduring, noting that this depends on the common understanding that the perpetual is considered necessary.

Key Terms
بين بالمعنى الأخص
بين بالمعنى الأعم
غير بين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of 'bayyin bil-ma‘nā al-akhass'?
A) Evenness of four.
B) Heat for fire.
C) Loss of sight from blindness.
D) Writing potential in man.

Key Takeaway
The two senses of 'evident' represent different epistemic immediacy: one in conception, one in judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which sense of 'evident' applies to: (a) The concept of 'Creator' follows from the concept of 'created world'. (b) The judgment that the world is temporally originated follows from conceiving change and the argument of temporal origination. Justify your answers by referencing the definitions in the passage.

Objection Against Conflating Two Divisions
Addressing an objection about conflating two divisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that the author conflates two divisions and explain the commentator's defense based on shared terminology.

Core Idea
A single division may mask two distinct classifications when terms are ambiguous.

After establishing the two senses of 'evident', the commentator addresses an objection: the author's wording suggests only one division, but in reality there are two. The response highlights how common terminology can obscure logical structure. This lesson dissects that objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find where Burhan al-Din states 'فهذا التقسيم ههنا محل إيراد' (this division is a point of objection). The objection is that from the author's words, it seems 'evident' and 'non-evident' each have only one meaning, while in fact they have two distinct meanings.
The objection is that the division in the text appears singular but is actually two separate divisions.
2
Understand the defense
The commentator responds that the author's single division is actually two divisions (one for each sense), but because the terms 'evident' and 'non-evident' are used for both, the author presented them as one. The defense is that this is a rhetorical convenience, not a logical error.
The commentator points out that the author 'due to the common usage of the term' presented it as one.
3
Analyze the perpetual motion example
The text includes a discussion about the motion of the celestial sphere as an enduring concomitant. A proof is given that it is necessary because it cannot separate from its cause, but an objection says this would make perpetual equivalent to necessary, which is a philosophical subtlety.
This example illustrates how even enduring attributes (non-evident in the specific sense) might be considered necessary in the general sense due to their cause.
4
Evaluate the overall lesson
The key takeaway is that when a term is ambiguous, one must ensure that divisions based on that term are clearly separated to avoid conflation. In Islamic logic, this awareness prevents misunderstanding in later arguments, especially in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second division of the necessary concomitant (اللازم) into evident (بين) and non-evident (غير بين). The commentator first provides two meanings of 'evident': the specific sense (الأخص) where merely conceptualizing the necessitating thing yields the concept of the concomitant (e.g., blindness yields the concept of sight), and the general sense (الأعم) where conceptualizing both terms and their relation yields a judgment of necessity (e.g., evenness of four). For each, the non-evident is the opposite. The commentator then explains that this single division actually comprises two distinct divisions, one for each sense, but they share the same labels. Moving to examples, the commentator discusses the phrase 'it endures' (يدوم) with the motion of the celestial sphere as an enduring concomitant, and gives examples of quickly changing (blushing) and slowly changing (pallor) attributes. Then there is a critique: the author's wording suggests only one meaning of 'evident', but there are two. The response is that the author combined the divisions due to the common terminology. A further point is made about the relation between the two senses: the specific sense is not necessarily a subset of the general sense because judgment of necessity might require a middle term. The commentary ends with a philosophical discussion about whether the perpetual motion of the sphere is truly necessary or merely enduring, noting that this depends on the common understanding that the perpetual is considered necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the commentator's defense against the objection that the author conflates two divisions?
A) The author made an error in classification.
B) The division is actually one, not two.
C) The two divisions share the same labels, so the author presented them as one.
D) The objection is invalid because the author explicitly mentions two senses.

Key Takeaway
Ambiguity in terminology can lead to conflating distinct logical divisions; careful analysis is needed to uncover the underlying structure.

The Three Aspects of Every Universal
Definition of three types of universal and application to the five universals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logical, natural, and mental aspects of a universal and apply them to any of the five universals in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The same universal can be viewed as a logical concept, a natural reality, or a mental construct.

In the study of concepts (tasawwurat), the universal is a foundational tool. But what exactly is a 'universal'? The logician, the metaphysician, and the psychologist each look at it differently. This passage from the commentary on Tahdhib al-Mantiq clarifies three distinct aspects of every universal, and then shows how they apply to the five universals—genus, species, differentia, property, and general accident. By understanding these aspects, you will be able to navigate logical definitions with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Terms
Locate in the Arabic the three phrases: الكلي المنطقي, الكلي الطبيعي, الكلي العقلي. Note that they stem from the same universal but differ in consideration.
The matn states: 'مفهوم الكلي يسمى كليا منطقيا ومعروضه طبعيا والمجموع عقليا'.
2
Understand the Definition of Each
The commentary explains: logical universal is the concept itself without restriction to any matter; natural universal is what the concept applies to in external reality (e.g., man); mental universal is the combination of concept and subject in the mind (e.g., universal man).
The commentator emphasizes that the logical universal is the subject of logical questions.
3
Apply to the Five Universals
The author says: 'كما أن الكلي يكون منطقيا وطبعيا وعقليا كذلك الأنواع الخمسة'. Verify this by taking 'species' as an example: its concept is logical species, its external reality is natural species, its mental representation is mental species.
Check the example of 'species' in the passage: مفهوم النوع يسمى نوعا منطقيا.
4
Reflect on the Naming Objection
A question arises: why isn't the logical universal called 'mental' since it also exists in the mind? The answer: the reason for naming does not have to be universal. This highlights that each aspect has a distinct purpose.
The objection and response are in the last lines of the commentary.

خاتمة
الخمسة،
مفهوم الكلي يسمى "كليا منطقيا" ومعروضه "طبعيا" والمجموع "عقليا"، وكذا الأنواع كالشباب قوله مفهوم الكلى: أي ما يطلق عليه لفظ الكلي، يعني المفهوم الذي لا يمتنع فرض : صدقه على كثيرين يسمى كليا منطقيا؛ فإن المنطقي يقصد من الكلي هذا المعنى. قوله: ومعروضه أي ما يصدق عليه مفهوم الكلي كالإنسان والحيوان يسمى كليا طبعيا؛ لوجوده في الطبائع يعني في الخارج على ما سيجيء. قوله والمجموع المركب من هذا العارض والمعروض، كالإنسان الكلي والحيوان الكلي يسمي كليا عقليا؛ إذ لا وجود له إلا في العقل. قوله: وكذا الأنواع الخمسة: يعني كما أن الكلي يكون منطقيا وطبعيا وعقليا كذلك الأنواع الجنس والفصل والنوع والخاصة والعرض العام تجري في كل منها هذه الاعتبارات الثلاث، مثلا: مفهوم النوع، أعني الكلي المقول على كثيرين متفقين بالحقيقة في جواب "ما" هو "؟ يسمى نوعا منطقيا،
الخمسة
-يعني
كالشباب هذا أولى مما قال بعضهم: كالمشيب؛ فإن زواله إنما يكون بزوال الموضوع والمواريد منه الكهولة، فهذا المعنى ليس متعارفا عندهم. إسماعيل) مفهوم الكلي: مفهوم لفظ الكلي من غير اعتبار تقييده بمادة من المواد، وهذا المفهوم يقع موضوعا للمسائل المنطقية التي يبحث فيها عن المعقولات الثانية من حيث الإيصال، فهذا المفهوم هو الكلي المنطقي . (عبد) فإن المنطقي: بمعنى أنه يأخذ مفهوم الكليات من الكلي كالجنس والنوع والفصل من حيث هي بلا إشارة إلى مادة مخصوصة وارداً عليه الأحكام لتكون تلك الأحكام عامة شاملة الجميع ما صدق عليه مفهوم الكلي. شيخ الإسلام
كليا طبعيا لأنه طبيعة من الطبائع أي حقيقة من الحقائق أو لأنه موجود في الطبيعة أي في الخارج كما أشار
إليه بقوله: "لوجوده في الطبائع" يعني في الخارج، فالطبيعة لفظ مشترك في الحقيقة والخارج. (عبد) في الخارج: هذا إنما يظهر على مذهب القائلين بوجوده في الخارج، أما عند من يقول بعدمه فلا. إسماعيل) إذ لا وجود له إلخ: فإن قلت الكلي المنطقي أيضاً لا تحقق له إلا في العقل فلم لم يسم بهذا الاسم؟ قلت: وجه التسمية لا يجب أن يكون مطردا. إسماعيل)
Conclusion of the topic of concepts. The logical, natural, and mental universal. Conclusion. The five (universals). The concept of the universal is called 'logical universal', its subject is called 'natural universal', and the combination is called 'mental universal'. Likewise the types (such as) youth. His saying: 'the concept of the universal' i.e., that to which the word 'universal' is applied, meaning the concept that does not prevent the assumption of its truth for many; it is called logical universal, for the logician intends from 'universal' this meaning. His saying: 'its subject' i.e., that to which the concept of universal applies, like man and animal, is called natural universal, due to its existence in natures, meaning in the external, as will come. His saying: 'the combination' composed of this accident and subject, like 'universal man' and 'universal animal', is called mental universal, since it has no existence except in the mind. His saying: 'Likewise the five types' means: just as the universal is logical, natural, and mental, so also the types: genus, differentia, species, property, and general accident; each of these has these three aspects. For example, the concept of species, i.e., the universal predicated of many things differing in reality in answer to 'what is it?', is called logical species. The five. He means: 'like youth' – this is more appropriate than what some said, like senility, for its departure only occurs with the departure of the subject, and the sources of it are middle age; so this meaning is not customary among them. Isma'il. 'The concept of the universal': the concept of the word 'universal' without considering its restriction to any matter; this concept is the subject of logical questions in which secondary intelligibles are investigated in terms of leading to knowledge. So this concept is the logical universal. (Abd) 'For the logician': meaning he takes the concept of universals from the universal like genus, species, and differentia in themselves, without reference to a specific matter, applying judgments to them so that those judgments are general and cover everything that the concept of universal applies to. Shaykh al-Islam. 'Natural universal' because it is a nature among natures, i.e., a reality among realities, or because it exists in nature, i.e., in the external, as he indicated by his saying: 'due to its existence in natures', meaning in the external. So 'nature' is a word shared between reality and the external. (Abd) 'In the external': this only appears according to those who affirm its existence in the external; as for those who deny it, no. Isma'il. 'Since it has no existence', etc.: If you say: the logical universal also has no realization except in the mind, so why is it not called by this name? I say: The reason for naming does not have to be universal. Isma'il.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion on the three types of universal. It begins with a title indicating it is the conclusion of the section on concepts. The matn states that the concept of the universal is called a 'logical universal' because it is considered by logicians in itself. Its subject (what it is predicated of in external reality) is called the 'natural universal'. The combination of the concept and its subject in the mind is the 'mental universal'. The commentary explains each term: 'logical universal' is the concept of 'universal' without regard to any specific instance; the logician uses it as a tool for knowledge. 'Natural universal' is the reality (such as humanity or animality) that exists in external particular things. 'Mental universal' is the universal as it exists in the mind, like 'universal man'. Then the passage extends this to the five universals (genus, species, differentia, property, general accident): each has these three aspects. An example is given for species. There is a brief debate about the best example for a certain point, preferring 'youth' over 'senility'. Finally, additional commentary clarifies that 'nature' can refer to both the external reality and the essence, and that existence in the external is a matter of philosophical doctrine. A question is answered: why the logical universal is not called mental, since it too is in the mind? The answer is that the reason for naming need not be consistent across all cases.

Checkpoint
Which type of universal is the focus of logical analysis according to the passage?
A) The natural universal
B) The mental universal
C) The logical universal
D) All three equally

Key Takeaway
Every universal in Islamic logic has three aspects—logical, natural, and mental—and each of the five universals shares this tripartite structure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the universal 'shahada' (witness) in Islamic legal procedure. Write a brief analysis showing its logical universal aspect (the concept of a witness), its natural universal aspect (the real individuals who testify), and its mental universal aspect (the idea of a witness in the judge's mind). Then explain which aspect is relevant for the rule that a witness must be just ('adl).
?
For the universal 'jins' (genus), identify its three aspects using the definitions from this passage. Provide one sentence for each aspect.
